High Middle Ages">High medieval Scotland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the Late Middle Ages">Late medieval Scotland</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a Wales</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wales_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="Wales in the High Middle Ages">High medieval Wales</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wales_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Wales in Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="wrap" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_Isles#19th_century" title="History of the British Isles">Late modern period</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;text-align:left">\n<ul><li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">United Kingdom</a> (since 1707)\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="History of the United Kingdom during the First World War">First World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x931945" title="History of the United Kingdom">Second World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (political history)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (social history)</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="History of the Republic of Ireland">Late modern Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Isle_of_Man#Modern_period" title="History of the Isle of Man">Late modern Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><br />\n</p><p>\nThe <b>history of the United Kingdom</b> as a unified state can be treated as beginning in 1707 with the <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">political union</a> of the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> into a united kingdom called <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> Of this new <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> the historian <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama" title="Simon Schama">Simon Schama</a> said: </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What began as a hostile merger would end in a full partnership in the most powerful <a href="/wiki/Going_concern" title="Going concern">going concern</a> in the world... it was one of the most astonishing transformations in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p> The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a> added the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>.\n</p><p>The first decades were marked by <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_risings" title="Jacobite risings">Jacobite risings</a> which ended with defeat for the Stuart cause at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Culloden</a> in 1746. In 1763, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years%27_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years&#39; War">victory in the Seven Years\' War</a> led to the growth of the First British Empire. With the defeat by the United States, France and Spain in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_American_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="War of American Independence">War of American Independence</a>, Britain lost its 13 American colonies and rebuilt a <a href="/wiki/Second_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Second British Empire">Second British Empire</a> based in Asia and Africa.  As a result, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">British culture</a>, and its technological, political, constitutional, and linguistic influence, became worldwide.  Politically, the central event was the French Revolution And its Napoleonic aftermath, 1793\xe2\x80\x931815, which British elites saw as a profound threat, and worked energetically to form multiple coalitions that finally defeated Napoleon in 1815. The Tories, who came to power in 1783, remained in power (with a short interruption) until 1830. Forces of reform, often emanating from the Evangelical religious elements, opened decades of political reform that broadened the ballot, and opened the economy to free trade. The outstanding political leaders of the 19th century included Palmerston, Disraeli, Gladstone, and Salisbury.  Culturally the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> (Queen Victoria reigned 1837\xe2\x80\x931901) was a time of prosperity and dominant middle-class virtues when Britain dominated the world economy and maintained a generally peaceful century, 1815\xe2\x80\x931914. The First World War (1914\xe2\x80\x931918), in alliance with France, Russia and the United States, was a furious but ultimately successful total war with Germany. The resulting <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> was a favorite project in <a href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar Britain</a>. However, while the Empire remained strong, As did the London financial markets, the British industrial base began to slip behind Germany and especially the United States. Sentiments for peace were so strong that the nation supported appeasement of Hitler\'s Germany in the late 1930s, until the Nazi invasion of Poland in 1939 opened the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> 1939\xe2\x80\x9345, France, the Soviet Union the U.S. joined Britain as <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">the main Allied powers</a>. Britain and its Empire faced near defeat at several points, 1940\xe2\x80\x9342, but finally crushed Germany, Italy and Japan. \n</p><p>Britain was no longer a military or economic superpower, as seen in the Suez fiasco of 1956. Britain no longer had the wealth to maintain an empire, so it granted independence to almost all its possessions. The new states typically joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>.  Postwar years saw great hardships, alleviated somewhat by large-scale financial aid from the United States, and some from Canada. Prosperity returned in the 1950s. Meanwhile, in 1945\xe2\x80\x9350 the Labour Party built a welfare state, nationalized many industries, and created the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a>.  The UK took a strong stand against Communist expansion after 1945, playing a major role in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the formation of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> as an anti-Soviet military alliance with West Germany, France, the U.S., Canada and smaller countries.  NATO remains a powerful military coalition. The UK has been a leading member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> since its founding, as well as numerous other international organizaations.  In the 1990s, neoliberalism led to the privatisation of nationalized industries and significant deregulation of business affairs.  London\'s status as a world financial hub grew continuously. Since the 1990s large-scale <a href="/wiki/Devolution_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Devolution in the United Kingdom">devolution movements in Northern Ireland, Scotland and Wales</a> have decentralized political decision-making. Britain has wobbled back and forth on its economic relationships with Western Europe. It joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in 1973, thereby weakening economic ties with its Commonwealth. However, the <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> referendum in 2016 committed the UK to an exit from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>; negotiations are currently underway.\n</p><p>In 1922, Catholic Ireland seceded to become the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>; a day later, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> seceded from the Free State and returned to the United Kingdom. In 1927 the United Kingdom changed its formal title to the <i>United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland,</i><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> usually shortened to <i><a href="/wiki/British_Isles_(terminology)" class="mw-redirect" title="British Isles (terminology)">Britain</a></i> and (after 1945) to the <i>United Kingdom</i> or <i>UK</i>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div Wales</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-62"><a href="#21st_century"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">21st century</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-63"><a href="#War_in_Afghanistan_and_Iraq,_and_terrorist_attacks"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">War in Afghanistan and Iraq, and terrorist attacks</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-64"><a href="#Nationalist_government_in_Scotland"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Nationalist government in Scotland</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-65"><a href="#The_2008_economic_crisis"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">The 2008 economic crisis</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-66"><a href="#The_2010_coalition_government"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">The 2010 coalition government</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-67"><a href="#2014_Scottish_Independence_referendum"><span class="tocnumber">9.5</span> <span class="toctext">2014 Scottish Independence referendum</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-68"><a href="#2015_election"><span class="tocnumber">9.6</span> <span class="toctext">2015 election</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-69"><a href="#2016_EU_referendum"><span class="tocnumber">9.7</span> <span class="toctext">2016 EU referendum</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-70"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-71"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-72"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-73"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-74"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-75"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-76"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-77"><a href="#Scholarly_journals"><span class="tocnumber">14.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Scholarly journals</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-78"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Britain">Early Modern Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian era</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_the_Union">Birth of the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth of the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_formation_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the formation of the United Kingdom">History of the formation of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg/220px-Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg/330px-Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Articles_of_Union_1707.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Articles of Union with Scotland", 1707</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> came into being on 1 May 1707, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">political union</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> (which included <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>. This combined the two kingdoms into a single kingdom, and merged the two parliaments into a single <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">parliament of Great Britain</a>. <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> became the first monarch of the new Great Britain. Although now a single kingdom, certain institutions of Scotland and England remained separate, such as <a href="/wiki/Scots_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots Law">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. England and Scotland each also continued to have their own system of education.\n</p><p>\nMeanwhile, the long <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a> against France (1701\xe2\x80\x931714) was under way. It see-sawed back and forth until a more peace-minded government came to power in London and the treaties of <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Utrecht" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Utrecht">Utrecht</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rastatt" title="Treaty of Rastatt">Rastadt</a> in 1713\xe2\x80\x931714 ended the war. British historian <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">G. M. Trevelyan</a> argues: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>That Treaty [of Utrecht], which ushered in the stable and characteristic period of Eighteenth-Century civilization, marked the end of danger to Europe from the old French monarchy, and it marked a change of no less significance to the world at large,\xe2\x80\x94the maritime, commercial and financial supremacy of Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hanoverian_kings">Hanoverian kings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hanoverian kings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_I_Oval.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/George_I_Oval.jpg/220px-George_I_Oval.jpg" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/George_I_Oval.jpg/330px-George_I_Oval.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/George_I_Oval.jpg/440px-George_I_Oval.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_I_Oval.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>George I in 1714, by Godfrey Kneller</div></div></div>\n<p>The Stuart line died with Anne in 1714, although a die-hard faction with French support supported pretenders. The Elector of Hanover became king as <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">George I</a> (1714\xe2\x80\x931727). He paid more attention to Hanover and surrounded himself with Germans, making him an unpopular king.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> However he did build up the army and created a more stable political system in Britain and helped bring peace to northern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Jacobite factions seeking a Stuart restoration remained strong; they instigated a revolt in 1715\xe2\x80\x931716. The son of <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a> planned to invade England, but before he could do so, <a href="/wiki/John_Erskine,_22nd_Earl_of_Mar" class="mw-redirect" title="John Erskine, 22nd Earl of Mar">John Erskine, Earl of Mar</a>, launched an invasion from Scotland, which was easily defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a> (1727\xe2\x80\x931760) enhanced the stability of the constitutional system, with a government run by Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a> during the period 1730\xe2\x80\x9342.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He built up the <a href="/wiki/First_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="First British Empire">First British Empire</a>, strengthening the colonies in the Caribbean and North America. In coalition with the rising power Prussia, the United Kingdom defeated France in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a> (1756\xe2\x80\x931763), and won full control of Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_III" class="mw-redirect" title="George III">George III</a> (1760\xe2\x80\x931820) never visited Hanover, and spoke English as his first language. Reviled by Americans as a tyrant and the instigator of the American War of Independence, he was insane off and on after 1788, and his eldest son served as regent.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He was the last king to dominate government and politics, and his long reign is noted for losing the first British Empire in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (1783), as France sought revenge for its defeat in the Seven Years\' War by aiding the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The reign was notable for the building of a second empire based in India, Asia and Africa, the beginnings of the industrial revolution that made Britain an economic powerhouse, and above all the life and death struggle with the French, in the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> 1793\xe2\x80\x931802, which ended inconclusively with a short truce, and the epic <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> (1803\xe2\x80\x931815), which ended with the decisive defeat of Napoleon.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Sea_Bubble">South Sea Bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: South Sea Bubble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Entrepreneurs gradually extended the range of their business around the globe. The <a href="/wiki/South_Sea_Bubble" class="mw-redirect" title="South Sea Bubble">South Sea Bubble</a> was a business enterprise that exploded in scandal. The South Sea Company was a joint-stock company in London. Its ostensible object was to grant trade monopolies in South America; but its actual purpose was to renegotiate previous high-interest government loans amounting to \xc2\xa331&#160;million through market manipulation and speculation. It raised money four times in 1720 by issuing stock, which was purchased by about 8,000 investors. The share price kept soaring every day, from \xc2\xa3130 a share to \xc2\xa31,000, with insiders making huge paper profits. The Bubble collapsed overnight, ruining many speculators. Investigations showed bribes had reached into high places\xe2\x80\x94even to the king. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a> managed to wind it down with minimal political and economic damage, although some losers fled to exile or committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_Walpole">Robert Walpole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Robert Walpole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Robert Walpole is now generally regarded as the first Prime Minister, from, 1719\xe2\x80\x9342, and indeed he invented the role.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (September 2018)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Dubious" title="Talk:History of the United Kingdom">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> The term was applied to him by friends and foes alike by 1727. Historian Clayton Roberts summarizes his new functions: \n</p>\n<dl><dd>He monopolized the counsels of the King, he closely superintended the administration, he ruthlessly controlled patronage, and he led the predominant party in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Walpole was a master of the effective use of patronage, as were his two disciples who succeeded him as prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pelham" title="Henry Pelham">Henry Pelham</a> (1743\xe2\x80\x931754) and Pelham\'s brother the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pelham-Holles,_1st_Duke_of_Newcastle" title="Thomas Pelham-Holles, 1st Duke of Newcastle">Duke of Newcastle</a> (1754\xe2\x80\x931762).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Moralism.2C_benevolence_and_hypocrisy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Moralism,_benevolence_and_hypocrisy">Moralism, benevolence and hypocrisy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Moralism, benevolence and hypocrisy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Hypocrisy became a major topic in English political history in the early 18th century. The <a href="/wiki/Toleration_Act_1689" title="Toleration Act 1689">Toleration Act 1689</a> allowed for certain rights for religious minorities, but Protestant <a href="/wiki/Nonconformists" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformists">Nonconformists</a> (such as Congregationalists and Baptists) were still deprived of important rights, such as the right to hold office. Nonconformists who wanted to hold office ostentatiously took the Anglican sacrament once a year in order to avoid the restrictions. High Church Anglicans were outraged. They outlawed what they called "occasional conformity" in 1711 with the <a href="/wiki/Occasional_Conformity_Act_1711" title="Occasional Conformity Act 1711">Occasional Conformity Act 1711</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In the political controversies using sermons, speeches, and pamphlet wars, both high churchmen and Nonconformists attacked their opponents as insincere and hypocritical, as well as dangerously zealous, in contrast to their own moderation. This campaign of moderation versus zealotry peaked in 1709 during the impeachment trial of high church preacher <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sacheverell" title="Henry Sacheverell">Henry Sacheverell</a>. Historian Mark Knights argues that by its very ferocity, the debate may have led to more temperate and less hypercharged political discourse. "Occasional conformity" was restored by the Whigs when they returned to power in 1719.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English author <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Mandeville" title="Bernard Mandeville">Bernard Mandeville</a>\'s famous "<a href="/wiki/Fable_of_the_Bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Fable of the Bees">Fable of the Bees</a>" (1714) explored the nature of hypocrisy in contemporary European society. On one hand, Mandeville was a "moralist" heir to the French Augustinianism of the previous century, viewing sociability as a mere mask for vanity and pride. On the other, he was a "materialist" who helped found modern economics. He tried to demonstrate the universality of human appetites for corporeal pleasures. He argued that the efforts of self-seeking entrepreneurs are the basis of emerging commercial and industrial society, a line of thought that influenced <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> (1723\xe2\x80\x931790) and 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</a>. The tension between these two approaches modes ambivalences and contradictions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\xe2\x80\x94concerning the relative power of norms and interests, the relationship between motives and behaviours, and the historical variability of human cultures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">This sentence appears to have no main verb.</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From around 1750 to 1850, Whig aristocrats in England boasted of their special benevolence for the common people. They claimed to be guiding and counselling reform initiatives to prevent the outbreaks of popular discontent that caused instability and revolution across Europe. However Tory and radical critics accused the Whigs of hypocrisy\xe2\x80\x94alleging they were deliberately using the slogans of reform and democracy to boost themselves into power while preserving their precious aristocratic exclusiveness. Historian L.G. Mitchell defends the Whigs, pointing out that thanks to them radicals always had friends at the centre of the political elite, and thus did not feel as marginalised as in most of Europe. He points out that the debates on the 1832 Reform Bill showed that reformers would indeed receive a hearing at parliamentary level with a good chance of success.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, a steady stream of observers from the Continent commented on the English political culture. Liberal and radical observers noted the servility of the English lower classes, the obsession everyone had with rank and title, the extravagance of the aristocracy, a supposed anti-intellectualism, and a pervasive hypocrisy that extended into such areas as social reform. There were not so many conservative visitors. They praised the stability of English society, its ancient constitution, and reverence for the past; they ignored the negative effects of industrialisation.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare_and_finance">Warfare and finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Warfare and finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From 1700 to 1850, Britain was involved in 137 wars or rebellions. It maintained a relatively large and expensive <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, along with a small standing army. When the need arose for soldiers it hired mercenaries or financed allies who fielded armies. The rising costs of warfare forced a shift in the sources of government financing, from the income from royal agricultural estates and special imposts and taxes to reliance on customs and excise taxes; and, after 1790, an income tax. Working with bankers in the City, the government raised large loans during wartime and paid them off in peacetime. The rise in taxes amounted to 20% of national income, but the private sector benefited from the increase in economic growth. The demand for war supplies stimulated the industrial sector, particularly naval supplies, munitions and textiles, which gave Britain an advantage in international trade during the postwar years.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> polarised British political opinion in the 1790s, with conservatives outraged at the killing of the king, the expulsion of the nobles, and the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>. Britain was at war against France almost continuously from 1793 until the final defeat of Napoleon in 1815. Conservatives castigated every radical opinion in Britain as "Jacobin" (in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Jacobin" title="Jacobin">leaders of the Terror</a>), warning that radicalism threatened an upheaval of British society. The Anti-Jacobin sentiment, well expressed by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Jacobin" title="Anti-Jacobin">many popular writers</a> was strongest among the landed gentry and the upper classes.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Empire">British Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: British Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clive.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/220px-Clive.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/330px-Clive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/440px-Clive.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1870" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clive.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lord_Clive" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Clive">Lord Clive</a> meeting with <a href="/wiki/Mir_Jafar" title="Mir Jafar">Mir Jafar</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hayman" title="Francis Hayman">Francis Hayman</a> (c. 1762).</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a>, which began in 1756, was the first war waged on a global scale, fought in Europe, India, North America, the Caribbean, the Philippines and coastal Africa. The signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris (1763)</a> had important consequences for Britain and its empire. In North America, France\'s future as a colonial power there was effectively ended with the ceding of <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> to Britain (leaving a sizeable French-speaking population under British control) and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">Louisiana</a> to Spain. Spain ceded <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> to Britain. In India, the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars#Third_Carnatic_War_(1756\xe2\x80\x931763)" title="Carnatic Wars">Carnatic War</a> had left France still in control of its <a href="/wiki/French_India" title="French India">enclaves</a> but with military restrictions and an obligation to support British client states, effectively leaving the future of India to Britain. <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years%27_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years&#39; War">The British victory over France</a> in the Seven Years\' War therefore left Britain as the world\'s dominant colonial power.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1760s and 1770s, relations between the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> and Britain became increasingly strained, primarily because of opposition to Parliament\'s repeated attempts to tax American colonists without their consent.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Disagreement turned to violence and in 1775 the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> began. In 1776 the Patriots expelled royal officials and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">declared the independence of the United States of America</a>. After capturing a British invasion army in 1777, the US formed an alliance with France (and in turn Spain aided France), evening out the military balance. The British army controlled only a handful of coastal cities. 1780\xe2\x80\x9381 was a low point for Britain. Taxes and deficits were high, government corruption was pervasive, and the war in America was entering its sixth year with no apparent end in sight. The <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Riots" title="Gordon Riots">Gordon Riots</a> erupted in London during the spring of 1781, in response to increased concessions to Catholics by Parliament. In October 1781 <a href="/wiki/Lord_Cornwallis" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Cornwallis">Lord Cornwallis</a> surrendered his army at <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Yorktown, Virginia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> was signed in 1783, formally terminating the war and recognising the independence of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg/220px-Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg/330px-Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg/440px-Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_General_Burgoyne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British general John Burgoyne surrenders at Saratoga (1777), painting by John Trumbull 1822</div></div></div>\n<p>The loss of the Thirteen Colonies, at the time Britain\'s most populous colonies, marked the transition between the "first" and "second" empires,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> in which Britain shifted its attention to Asia, the Pacific and later Africa. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Wealth_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Wealth of Nations">Wealth of Nations</a></i>, published in 1776, had argued that colonies were redundant, and that <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> should replace the old <a href="/wiki/Mercantilist" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercantilist">mercantilist</a> policies that had characterised the first period of colonial expansion, dating back to the protectionism of Spain and Portugal. The growth of trade between the newly independent United States and Britain after 1783<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> confirmed Smith\'s view that political control was not necessary for economic success.\n</p><p>During its first 100 years of operation, the focus of the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> had been trade, not the building of an empire in India. Company interests turned from trade to territory during the 18th century as the Mughal Empire declined in power and the British East India Company struggled with its French counterpart, the <i><a href="/wiki/French_East_India_Company" title="French East India Company">La Compagnie fran\xc3\xa7aise des Indes orientales</a></i>, during the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars" title="Carnatic Wars">Carnatic Wars</a> of the 1740s and 1750s. The British, led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Clive" title="Robert Clive">Robert Clive</a>, defeated the French and their Indian allies in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a>, leaving the Company in control of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> and a major military and political power in India. In the following decades it gradually increased the size of the territories under its control, either ruling directly or indirectly via local puppet rulers under the threat of force of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Army" title="Indian Army">Indian Army</a>, 80% of which was composed of native Indian <a href="/wiki/Sepoys" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepoys">sepoys</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cookroutes.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Cookroutes.png/220px-Cookroutes.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Cookroutes.png/330px-Cookroutes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Cookroutes.png/440px-Cookroutes.png 2x" data-file-width="643" data-file-height="430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cookroutes.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Voyages of the explorer James Cook</div></div></div>\n<p>On 22 August 1770, <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> discovered the eastern coast of Australia<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> while on a scientific <a href="/wiki/First_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="First voyage of James Cook">voyage</a> to the South Pacific. In 1778, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Banks" title="Joseph Banks">Joseph Banks</a>, Cook\'s botanist on the voyage, presented evidence to the government on the suitability of <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a> for the establishment of a penal settlement, and in 1787 the first shipment of <a href="/wiki/Convictism_in_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Convictism in Australia">convicts</a> set sail, arriving in 1788.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1921.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/220px-British_Empire_1921.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/330px-British_Empire_1921.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/440px-British_Empire_1921.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1921.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Empire in 1921</div></div></div>\n<p>The British government had somewhat mixed reactions to the outbreak of the French Revolution in 1789, and when war broke out on the Continent in 1792, it initially remained neutral. But the following January, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a> was beheaded. This combined with a threatened invasion of the Netherlands by France spurred Britain to declare war. For the next 23 years, the two nations were at war except for a short period in 1802\xe2\x80\x931803. Britain alone among the nations of Europe never submitted to or formed an alliance with France. Throughout the 1790s, the British repeatedly defeated the navies of France and its allies, but were unable to perform any significant land operations. An Anglo-Russian invasion of the Netherlands in 1799 accomplished little except the capture of the Dutch fleet.\n</p><p>At the threshold to the 19th century, Britain was challenged again by France under <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, in a struggle that, unlike previous wars, represented a contest of ideologies between the two nations: the constitutional monarchy of Great Britain versus the liberal principles of the French Revolution ostensibly championed by the Napoleonic empire.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> It was not only Britain\'s position on the world stage that was threatened: Napoleon threatened invasion of Britain itself, and with it, a fate similar to the countries of continental Europe that his armies had overrun.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1800_to_1837">1800 to 1837</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1800 to 1837">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian era</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Regency" class="mw-redirect" title="British Regency">British Regency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_society#Georgian_society" class="mw-redirect" title="History of British society">History of British society \xc2\xa7&#160;Georgian society</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Ireland">Union with Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Union with Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/220px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/330px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/440px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">Flag of the United Kingdom</a> is based on the flags of <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_England" title="Flag of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saltire" title="Saltire">saltire</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St_Patrick" title="Order of St Patrick">Order of St Patrick</a> (not the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">flag of Ireland</a>)</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_issue_in_British_politics" title="Irish issue in British politics">Irish issue in British politics</a></div>\n<p>On 1 January 1801, the first day of the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a> joined to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>.\n</p><p>The legislative union of Great Britain and Ireland was brought about by the Act of Union 1800, creating the "<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>". The Act was passed in both the Parliament of Great Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Parliament of Ireland</a>, dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a> and lacking representation of the country\'s <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> population. Substantial majorities were achieved, and according to contemporary documents this was assisted by <a href="/wiki/Bribery" title="Bribery">bribery</a> in the form of the awarding of <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peerages</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_honours_system" class="mw-redirect" title="British honours system">honours</a> to opponents to gain their votes.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Under the terms of the merger, the separate Parliaments of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Ireland</a> were abolished, and replaced by a united <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>. Ireland thus became an integral part of the United Kingdom, sending around 100 MPs to the House of Commons at Westminster and 28 <a href="/wiki/Representative_peers" class="mw-redirect" title="Representative peers">representative peers</a> to the House of Lords, elected from among their number by the Irish peers themselves, except that Roman Catholic peers were not permitted to take their seats in the Lords. Part of the trade-off for the Irish Catholics was to be the granting of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a>, which had been fiercely resisted by the all-Anglican Irish Parliament. However, this was blocked by <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a>, who argued that emancipating the Roman Catholics would breach his <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Oath" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation Oath">Coronation Oath</a>. The Roman Catholic hierarchy had endorsed the Union. However the decision to block Catholic Emancipation fatally undermined the appeal of the Union.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleonic_wars">Napoleonic wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Napoleonic wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_United_Kingdom_in_the_Napoleonic_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="The United Kingdom in the Napoleonic Wars">The United Kingdom in the Napoleonic Wars</a></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition" title="War of the Second Coalition">War of the Second Coalition</a> (1799\xe2\x80\x931801), Britain occupied most of the French and Dutch colonies (the Netherlands had been a satellite of France since 1796), but tropical diseases claimed the lives of over 40,000 troops. When the Treaty of Amiens created a pause, Britain was forced to return most of the colonies. In May 1803, war was declared again. Napoleon\'s plans to invade Britain failed due to the inferiority of his navy, and in 1805, Lord Nelson\'s fleet decisively defeated the French and Spanish at Trafalgar, which was the last significant naval action of the Napoleonic Wars.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/220px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/330px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/440px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="897" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The British <a href="/wiki/HMS_Sandwich_(1759)" title="HMS Sandwich (1759)">HMS&#160;<i>Sandwich</i></a> fires to the French <a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">flagship</a> <a href="/wiki/French_ship_Bucentaure_(1803)" class="mw-redirect" title="French ship Bucentaure (1803)">French ship&#160;<i>Bucentaure</i></a> (completely dismasted) into battle off <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">Trafalgar</a>. The <i>Bucentaure</i> also fights <a href="/wiki/HMS_Victory" title="HMS Victory">HMS&#160;<i>Victory</i></a> (behind her) and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Temeraire_(1798)" title="HMS Temeraire (1798)">HMS&#160;<i>Temeraire</i></a> (left side of the picture). In fact, HMS <i>Sandwich</i> never fought at Trafalgar, it\'s a mistake from <a href="/wiki/Auguste_%C3%89tienne_Fran%C3%A7ois_Mayer" title="Auguste \xc3\x89tienne Fran\xc3\xa7ois Mayer">Auguste Mayer</a>, the painter.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1806, Napoleon issued the series of <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Decree" title="Berlin Decree">Berlin Decrees</a>, which brought into effect the <a href="/wiki/Continental_System" title="Continental System">Continental System</a>. This policy aimed to weaken the British export economy closing French-controlled territory to its trade. Napoleon hoped that isolating Britain from the Continent would end its economic dominance. It never succeeded in its objective. Britain possessed the greatest industrial capacity in Europe, and its mastery of the seas allowed it to build up considerable economic strength through trade to its possessions from its rapidly expanding new Empire. Britain\'s naval supremacy meant that France could never enjoy the peace necessary to consolidate its control over Europe, and it could threaten neither the home islands nor the main British colonies.\n</p><p>The Spanish uprising in 1808 at last permitted Britain to gain a foothold on the Continent. The Duke of Wellington and his army of British and Portuguese gradually pushed the French out of Spain and in early 1814, as Napoleon was being driven back in the east by the Prussians, Austrians, and Russians, Wellington invaded southern France. After Napoleon\'s surrender and exile to the island of Elba, peace appeared to have returned, but when he escaped back into France in 1815, the British and their allies had to fight him again. The armies of Wellington and Von Blucher defeated Napoleon once and for all at Waterloo.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Financing_the_war">Financing the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Financing the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A key element in British success was its ability to mobilize the nation\'s industrial and financial resources and apply them to defeating France. With a population of 16 million Britain was barely half the size of France with 30&#160;million. In terms of soldiers the French numerical advantage was offset by British subsidies that paid for a large proportion of the Austrian and Russian soldiers, peaking at about 450,000 in 1813.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Most important, the British national output remained strong and the well-organized business sector channeled products into what the military needed. The system of smuggling finished products into the continent undermined French efforts to ruin the British economy by cutting off markets. The British budget in 1814 reached \xc2\xa366&#160;million, including \xc2\xa310&#160;million for the Navy, \xc2\xa340&#160;million for the Army, \xc2\xa310&#160;million for the Allies, and \xc2\xa338&#160;million as interest on the national debt. The national debt soared to \xc2\xa3679&#160;million, more than double the GDP. It was willingly supported by hundreds of thousands of investors and tax payers, despite the higher taxes on land and a new income tax. The whole cost of the war came to \xc2\xa3831&#160;million. By contrast the French financial system was inadequate and Napoleon\'s forces had to rely in part on requisitions from conquered lands.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon also attempted economic warfare against Britain, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Decree" title="Berlin Decree">Berlin Decree</a> of 1806. It forbade the import of British goods into European countries allied with or dependent upon France, and installed the <a href="/wiki/Continental_System" title="Continental System">Continental System</a> in Europe. All connections were to be cut, even the mail. British merchants smuggled in many goods and the Continental System was not a powerful weapon of economic war.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> There was some damage to Britain, especially in 1808 and 1811, but its control of the oceans helped ameliorate the damage. Even more damage was done to the economies of France and its allies, which lost a useful trading partner.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Angry governments gained an incentive to ignore the Continental System, which led to the weakening of Napoleon\'s coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_1812_with_United_States">War of 1812 with United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: War of 1812 with United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_(1812).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/220px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/330px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/440px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="967" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_(1812).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Signing of the Treaty of Ghent (December 1812) with the U.S. diplomats</div></div></div>\n<p>Simultaneous with the Napoleonic Wars, trade disputes and British impressment of American sailors led to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> with the United States. The "second war of independence" for the American, it was little noticed in Britain, where all attention was focused on the struggle with France. The British could devote few resources to the conflict until the fall of Napoleon in 1814. American <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a> also inflicted a series of embarrassing defeats on the British navy, which was short on manpower due to the conflict in Europe. A stepped-up war effort that year brought about some successes such as the burning of Washington, but many influential voices such as the Duke of Wellington argued that an outright victory over the US was impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Peace was agreed to at the end of 1814, but <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, unaware of this, won a great victory over the British at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a> in January 1815 (news took several weeks to cross the Atlantic before the advent of steam ships). Ratification of the Treaty of Ghent ended the war in February 1815. The major result was the permanent defeat of the Indian allies the British had counted upon. The US-Canada border was demilitarised by both countries, and peaceful trade resumed, although worries of an American conquest of Canada persisted into the 1860s.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Postwar_reaction:_1815.E2.80.9322"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_reaction:_1815\xe2\x80\x9322">Postwar reaction: 1815\xe2\x80\x9322</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Postwar reaction: 1815\xe2\x80\x9322">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The postwar era was a time of economic depression, poor harvests, growing inflation, and high unemployment among returning soldiers.  As industrialisation progressed, Britain was more urban and less rural, and power shifted accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  The dominant Tory leadership, based in the declining rural sector, was fearful, reactionary and repressive. Tories feared the possible emergence of radicals who might be conspiring to emulate the dreaded <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.  In reality the violent radical element was small and weak; there were a handful of small conspiracies involving men with few followers and careless security; they were quickly suppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  Techniques of repression included the suspension of Habeas Corpus in 1817 (allowing the government to arrest and hold suspects without cause or trial).  Sidmouth\'s Gagging Acts of 1817 heavily muzzled the opposition newspapers; the reformers switched to pamphlets and sold 50,000 a week.   In reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Peterloo massacre">Peterloo massacre</a> of 1819, the Liverpool government passed the "<a href="/wiki/Six_Acts" title="Six Acts">Six Acts</a>" in 1819. They prohibited drills and military exercises; facilitated warrants for the search for weapons; outlawed public meetings of more than 50 people, including meetings to organize petitions; put heavy penalties on blasphemous and seditious publications; imposing a fourpenny stamp act on many pamphlets to cut down the flow on news and criticism. Offenders could be harshly punished including exile in Australia. In practice the laws were designed to deter troublemakers and reassure conservatives; they were not often used.  By the end of the 1820s, along with a general economic recovery, many of these repressive laws were repealed and in 1828 new legislation guaranteed the civil rights of religious dissenters.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A weak ruler as regent (1811\xe2\x80\x9320) and king (1820\xe2\x80\x9330), <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> let his ministers take full charge of government affairs, playing a far lesser role than his father, George III. The principle now became established that the king accepts as prime minister the person who wins a majority in the House of Commons, whether the king personally favors him or not. His governments, with little help from the king, presided over victory in the Napoleonic Wars, negotiated the peace settlement, and attempted to deal with the social and economic malaise that followed.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> His brother <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> ruled (1830\xe2\x80\x9337), but was little involved in politics. His reign saw several reforms: the <a href="/wiki/Poor_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor law">poor law</a> was updated, <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a> restricted, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">slavery abolished</a> in nearly all the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, and, most important, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> refashioned the British electoral system.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were no major wars until the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of 1853\xe2\x80\x9356.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> While Prussia, Austria, and Russia, as absolute monarchies, tried to suppress liberalism wherever it might occur, the British came to terms with new ideas. Britain intervened in Portugal in 1826 to defend a constitutional government there and recognising the independence of Spain\'s American colonies in 1824.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> British merchants and financiers, and later railway builders, played major roles in the economies of most Latin American nations.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The British intervened in 1827 on the side of the Greeks, who had been waging a war of independence against the Ottoman Empire since 1824.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whig_reforms_of_the_1830s">Whig reforms of the 1830s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Whig reforms of the 1830s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig Party</a> recovered its strength and unity by supporting moral reforms, especially the reform of the electoral system, the abolition of slavery and emancipation of the Catholics. <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a> was secured in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829">Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829</a>, which removed the most substantial restrictions on Roman Catholics in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-L._Woodward,_1938_pp_325\xe2\x80\x9330_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L._Woodward,_1938_pp_325\xe2\x80\x9330-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Whigs became champions of Parliamentary reform. They made <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Lord Grey</a> prime minister 1830\xe2\x80\x931834, and the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> became their signature measure. It broadened the franchise slightly and ended the system of <a href="/wiki/Rotten_and_pocket_boroughs" title="Rotten and pocket boroughs">rotten and pocket boroughs</a> (where elections were controlled by powerful families), and gave seats to new industrial centres.  The aristocracy continued to dominate the government, the Army and Royal Navy, and high society.<sup id="cite_ref-L._Woodward,_1938_pp_325\xe2\x80\x9330_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L._Woodward,_1938_pp_325\xe2\x80\x9330-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> After parliamentary investigations demonstrated the horrors of child labour, limited reforms were passed in 1833.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartism</a> emerged after the 1832 Reform Bill failed to give the vote to the working class. Activists denounced the \'betrayal\' of the working class and the \'sacrificing\' of their interests by the \'misconduct\' of the government. In 1838, Chartists issued the People\'s Charter demanding manhood suffrage, equal sized election districts, voting by ballots, payment of MPs (so poor men could serve), annual Parliaments, and abolition of property requirements. Elites saw the movement as pathological, so the Chartists were unable to force serious constitutional debate. Historians see Chartism as both a continuation of the 18th-century fight against corruption and as a new stage in demands for democracy in an industrial society.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1832, Parliament abolished slavery in the Empire with the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">Slavery Abolition Act 1833</a>. The government purchased the slaves for \xc2\xa320,000,000 (the money went to rich plantation owners who mostly lived in England), and freed the slaves, especially those in the Caribbean sugar islands.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Prime Ministers of the period included: <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wyndham_Grenville,_1st_Baron_Grenville" class="mw-redirect" title="William Wyndham Grenville, 1st Baron Grenville">Lord Grenville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Portland" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Portland">Duke of Portland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Perceval" title="Spencer Perceval">Spencer Perceval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jenkinson,_2nd_Earl_of_Liverpool" title="Robert Jenkinson, 2nd Earl of Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_John_Robinson,_1st_Viscount_Goderich" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick John Robinson, 1st Viscount Goderich">Lord Goderich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Lord Grey</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Victorian_era">Victorian era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Victorian era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/200px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" width="200" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/300px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/400px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2729" data-file-height="3851" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Victoria (1837\xe2\x80\x931901)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Victoria</a> ascended the throne in 1837 at age 18. Her long reign until 1901 saw Britain reach the zenith of its economic and political power. Exciting new technologies such as steam ships, railroads, photography, and telegraphs appeared, making the world much faster-paced. Britain again remained mostly inactive in Continental politics, and it was not affected by the wave of revolutions in 1848. The Victorian era saw the fleshing out of the second <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Scholars debate whether the Victorian period\xe2\x80\x94as defined by a variety of sensibilities and political concerns that have come to be associated with the Victorians\xe2\x80\x94actually begins with her coronation or the earlier passage of the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>. The era was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Regency_era" title="Regency era">Regency era</a> and succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwardian period">Edwardian period</a>.\n</p><p>Historians like Bernard Porter have characterized the mid-Victorian era, (1850\xe2\x80\x931870) as Britain\'s \'Golden Years.\'.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> There was peace and prosperity, as the national income per person grew by half. Much of the prosperity was due to the increasing industrialization, especially in textiles and machinery, as well as to the worldwide network of trade and engineering that produce profits for British merchants and experts from across the globe. There was peace abroad (apart from the short Crimean war, 1854\xe2\x80\x9356), and social peace at home.  Reforms in industrial conditions were set by Parliament.  For example, in 1842, the nation was scandalized by the use of children in coal mines. The <a href="/wiki/Mines_and_Collieries_Act_1842" title="Mines and Collieries Act 1842">Mines Act of 1842</a> banned employment of girls and boys under ten years old from working underground in coal mines.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  Opposition to the new order melted away, says Porter. The Chartist movement, peaked as a democratic movement among the working class in 1848; its leaders moved to other pursuits, such as trade unions and cooperative societies. The working class ignored foreign agitators like Karl Marx in their midst, and joined in celebrating the new prosperity. Employers typically were paternalistic, and generally recognized the trade unions.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Companies provided their employees with welfare services ranging from housing, schools and churches, to libraries, baths, and gymnasia. Middle-class reformers did their best to assist the working classes aspire to middle-class norms of \'respectability.\' \n</p><p>There was a spirit of libertarianism, says Porter, as people felt they were free. Taxes were very low, and government restrictions were minimal. There were still problem areas, such as occasional riots, especially those motivated by anti-Catholicism. Society was still ruled by the aristocracy and the gentry, which controlled high government offices, both houses of Parliament, the church, and the military. Becoming a rich businessman was not as prestigious as inheriting a title and owning a landed estate. Literature was doing well, but the fine arts languished as the Great Exhibition of 1851 showcased Britain\'s industrial prowess rather than its sculpture, painting or music.
