The educational system was mediocre; the capstone universities (outside Scotland) were likewise mediocre.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\nHistorian <a href="/wiki/Llewellyn_Woodward" title="Llewellyn Woodward">Llewellyn Woodward</a> has concluded:<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>For leisure or work, for getting or spending, England was a better country in 1879 than in 1815. The scales were less weighted against the weak, against women and children, and against the poor. There was greater movement, and less of the fatalism of an earlier age. The public conscience was more instructed, and the content of liberty was being widened to include something more than freedom from political constraint.... Yet England in 1871 was by no means an earthly paradise. The housing and conditions of life of the working class in town &amp; country were still a disgrace to an age of plenty.</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_cultural_history">Social and cultural history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social and cultural history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)">International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_trade_imperialism">Free trade imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Free trade imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Great London Exhibition of 1851 clearly demonstrated Britain\'s dominance in engineering and industry; that lasted until the rise of the United States and Germany in the 1890s. Using the imperial tools of free trade and financial investment,<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Semmel_1970_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Semmel_1970-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> it exerted major influence on many countries outside Europe, especially in Latin America and Asia. Thus Britain had both a formal Empire based on British rule and an informal one based on the British pound.<sup id="cite_ref-McLean_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Russia.2C_France_and_the_Ottoman_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia,_France_and_the_Ottoman_Empire">Russia, France and the Ottoman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Russia, France and the Ottoman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Question" title="Eastern Question">Eastern Question</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)">International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)</a></div>\n<p>One nagging fear was the possible collapse of the Ottoman Empire. It was well understood that a collapse of that country would set off a scramble for its territory and possibly plunge Britain into war. To head that off Britain sought to keep the Russians from occupying Constantinople and taking over the Bosporous Straits, as well as from threatening India via Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 1853, Britain and France intervened in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> and defeated Russia at a very high cost in casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In the 1870s the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> blocked Russia from imposing the harsh Treaty of San Stefano on the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Despite its alliance with the French in the Crimean War, Britain viewed the Second Empire of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> with some distrust, especially as the emperor constructed ironclad warships and began returning France to a more active foreign policy.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Civil_War">American Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: American Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_and_the_American_Civil_War" title="United Kingdom and the American Civil War">United Kingdom and the American Civil War</a></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861\xe2\x80\x931865), British leaders personally disliked American republicanism and favoured the more aristocratic Confederacy, as it had been a major source of cotton for textile mills. Prince Albert was effective in defusing a <a href="/wiki/Trent_Affair" title="Trent Affair">war scare in late 1861</a>. The British people, who depended heavily on American food imports, generally favoured the United States. What little cotton was available came from New York, as the blockade by the US Navy shut down 95% of Southern exports to Britain. In September 1862, Britain (along with France) contemplated stepping in and negotiating a peace settlement, which could only mean war with the United States. But in the same month, US president <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> announced the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> would be issued in January 1863 making abolition of slavery in the Confederacy a war goal. Since support of the Confederacy now meant support for slavery, there was no longer any possibility of European intervention. However, the British working class were quite overwhelmingly pro-Union. In the end, although Britain could survive without Southern cotton, the North\'s meat and grain was more important to feed the UK\'s urban population, especially as a series of bad harvests had affected British agriculture in the late 1850s to early 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, the British sold arms to both sides, built <a href="/wiki/Blockade_runner" title="Blockade runner">blockade runners</a> for a lucrative trade with the Confederacy, and surreptitiously allowed warships to be built for the Confederacy. The warships caused a major diplomatic row that was resolved in the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims">Alabama Claims</a> in 1872, in the Americans\' favour.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_expands">Empire expands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Empire expands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1867, Britain united most of its North American colonies as the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominion of Canada">Dominion of Canada</a>, giving it self-government and responsibility for its internal affairs. Britain handled foreign policy and defence. The second half of the 19th century saw a major expansion of Britain\'s colonial empire in Asia and Africa as well as the Pacific . In the "<a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>", the boast was having the Union Jack flying from "Cairo to Cape Town." Britain defended its empire with the world\'s dominant navy, and a small professional army. It was the only power in Europe to have no conscription.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rise of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> after 1871 posed a new challenge, for it (along with the United States) threatened to take Britain\'s place as the world\'s foremost industrial power. Germany acquired a number of colonies in Africa and the Pacific, but Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> succeeded in achieving general peace through his balance of power strategy. When <a href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="William II, German Emperor">William II</a> became emperor in 1888, he discarded Bismarck, began using bellicose language, and planned to build a navy to rival Britain\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boer_War">Boer War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Boer War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a></div>\n<p>Ever since Britain had taken control of South Africa from the Netherlands in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, it had run afoul of the Dutch settlers who further away and created two republics of their own. The British imperial vision called for control over the new countries and the Dutch-speaking "Boers" (or "<a href="/wiki/Afrikaners" title="Afrikaners">Afrikaners</a>") fought back in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">War in 1899\xe2\x80\x931902</a>. British historian <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts_(historian)" title="Andrew Roberts (historian)">Andrew Roberts</a> argues that the Boers insisted on keeping full control of both their two small republics, allowing no role whatever for nonwhites, and distinctly limited roles for British and other European settlers. These "<a href="/wiki/Uitlander" title="Uitlander">Uitlanders</a>" with the base of the economy, and paid 80 percent of the taxes, and had no vote. The Transvaal was in no sense a democracy, argues Roberts, for no black, Britain, Catholic or Jew was allowed to vote or hold any office. Johannesburg was the business center, with 50,000 primarily British residents, but was not permitted any local government. The English language was banned in official proceedings; no public meetings were permitted; newspapers were closed down arbitrarily; and full citizenship was technically possible but quite rare. Roberts says President <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kruger" title="Paul Kruger">Paul Kruger</a> "ran a tight, tough, quasi-police state from his state capital, Pretoria." the British government officially protested; while theoretically recognizing the Transvaal\'s right to manage its internal affairs, cabinet member <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> detailed the many ways how Uitlanders were mistreated as second-class non-citizens, despite their essential role in producing prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The Boer response to the British pressure was to declare war on 20 October 1899. The 410,000 Boers were massively outnumbered, but amazingly they waged a successful guerrilla war, which gave the British regulars a difficult fight. The Boers were landlocked and did not have access to outside help.  The weight of numbers, superior equipment, and often brutal tactics eventually brought about a British victory.  To defeat the guerrillas, the British rounded up their women and children into concentration camps, where many died of disease. World outrage focused on the camps, led by a large faction of the Liberal Party in Britain. However, the United States gave its support. The Boer republics were merged into <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> in 1910; it had internal self-government but its foreign policy was controlled by London and was an integral part of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The unexpectedly great difficulty in defeating the Boers forced a reevaluation of British policy. In military terms, it was clear that the Cardwell reforms have been inadequate. The call to establish a general staff to control military operations had been shelved by the Duke of Cambridge, himself a royal with enormous authority. It took a five more years to set up a general staff and other Army reforms, under the administration of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Haldane" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Haldane">Lord Haldane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Navy was now threatened by Germany. Britain responded by a massive building program launched in 1904 by the highly controversial <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">First Sea Lord, Sir John Fisher</a>. He launched the <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(1906)" title="HMS Dreadnought (1906)">HMS Dreadnought</a></i> in 1906. It was the first modern battleship based on new armour. new propulsion, new guns and gunnery that made all other warships obsolete.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\nThe Boer war demonstrated that Britain was not loved around the world\xe2\x80\x94it had more enemies than friends and its policy of "<a href="/wiki/Splendid_isolation" title="Splendid isolation">splendid isolation</a>" was one of high risk. It needed new friends. It made a military alliance with Japan, and buried old controversies to forge a close relationship with the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_trade_imperialism_2">Free trade imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Free trade imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Britain in addition to taking control of new territories, developed an enormous power in economic and financial affairs in numerous independent countries, especially in Latin America and Asia. It lent money, built railways, and engaged in trade. The Great London Exhibition of 1851 clearly demonstrated Britain\'s dominance in engineering, communications and industry; that lasted until the rise of the United States and Germany in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Semmel_1970_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Semmel_1970-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLean_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1890\xe2\x80\x931902 under Salisbury Britain promoted a policy of <a href="/wiki/Splendid_isolation" title="Splendid isolation">Splendid isolation</a> with no formal allies.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland_and_Home_Rule">Ireland and Home Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ireland and Home Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Irish_issue_in_British_politics" title="Irish issue in British politics">Irish issue in British politics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine (Ireland)</a></div>\n<p>Part of the agreement which led to the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a> stipulated that the Penal Laws in Ireland were to be repealed and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a> granted. However, King George III blocked emancipation. A campaign under <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a> led to the concession of Catholic Emancipation in 1829, allowing Catholics to sit in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Potato_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Potato blight">potato blight</a> hit Ireland in 1846, much of the rural population was left without food. Relief efforts were inadequate and hundreds of thousands died in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Hunger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Millions more migrated to England, or to North America. Ireland became permanently smaller in terms of population\n</p><p>In the 1870s new moderate nationalist movement was formed. As the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> it became a major factor in parliament under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Charles Stewart Parnell</a>.  Home Rule Bills introduced by Liberal Prime Minister Gladstone failed of passage, and split the Liberals. A significant <a href="/wiki/Irish_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Unionism">unionist minority</a> (largely based in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>), opposed Home Rule, fearing that a <a href="/wiki/Rome_Rule" title="Rome Rule">Catholic-Nationalist</a> parliament in Dublin would discriminate against them and would also hurt its industry.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Parliament passed laws in 1870, 1881, 1903 and 1909 that enabled most tenant farmers to purchase their lands, and lowered the rents of the others.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_2">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historically, the aristocracy was divided between Conservatives and Liberals. However, when Gladstone committed to home rule for Ireland, Britain\'s upper classes largely abandoned the Liberal party, giving the Conservatives a large permanent majority in the House of Lords. High Society in London, following the Queen, largely ostracized home rulers, and Liberal clubs were badly split. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> took a major element of upper-class supporters out of the Party and into a third party called "Liberal Unionism" that collaborated with and eventually merged into the Conservative party.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Gladstonian liberals in 1891 adopted <a href="/wiki/The_Newcastle_Programme" title="The Newcastle Programme">The Newcastle Programme</a> that included home rule for Ireland, disestablishment of the Church of England in Wales and Scotland, tighter controls on the sale of liquor, major extension of factory regulation, and various democratic political reforms. The Programme had a strong appeal to the Nonconformist middle-class Liberal element, which felt liberated by the departure of the aristocracy.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Queen_Victoria">Queen Victoria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Queen Victoria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Queen played a small role in politics, but became the iconic symbol of the nation, the empire, and proper, restrained behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Her strength lay in good common sense and directness of character; she expressed the qualities of the British nation which at that time made it preeminent in the world. As a symbol of domesticity, endurance and Empire, and as a woman holding the highest public office during an age when middle- and upper-class women were expected to beautify the home while men dominated the public sphere, Queen Victoria\'s influence has been enduring. Her success as ruler was due to the power of the self-images she successively portrayed of innocent young woman, devoted wife and mother, suffering and patient widow, and grandmotherly matriarch.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Palmerston">Palmerston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Palmerston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a> (1784\xe2\x80\x931865) dominated foreign policy for decades, through a period when Britain was at the height of its power, serving terms as both Foreign Secretary and Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> He became controversial at the time, and remains so today, for his aggressive bullying and his "liberal interventionist" policies. He was intensely patriotic; he used the Royal Navy to undermine the Atlantic slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disraeli">Disraeli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Disraeli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg 2x" data-file-width="287" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Disraeli</div></div></div>\n<p>Disraeli and Gladstone dominated the politics of the late 19th century, Britain\'s golden age of parliamentary government. They long were idolized, but historians in recent decades have become much more critical, especially regarding Disraeli.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Disraeli, 1st Earl of Beaconsfield">Benjamin Disraeli</a> (1804\xe2\x80\x931881), <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Benjamin_Disraeli" class="mw-redirect" title="Premiership of Benjamin Disraeli">prime minister 1868 and 1874\xe2\x80\x9380</a>, remains an iconic hero of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the British Conservative Party">Conservative Party</a>.  He played a central role in the creation the Party, defining its policies and its broad outreach. Disraeli is remembered for his influential voice in world affairs, his political battles with the Liberal leader William Gladstone, and his <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">one-nation conservatism</a> or "Tory democracy". He made the Conservatives the party most identified with the glory and power of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. He was born into a Jewish family, which became Episcopalian when he was 12 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Disraeli fought to protect established political, social, and religious values and elites; he emphasized the need for national leadership in response to radicalism, uncertainty, and materialism.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> He is especially known for his enthusiastic support for expanding and strengthening the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in India and Africa as the foundation of British greatness, in contrast to Gladstone\'s negative attitude toward imperialism. Gladstone denounced Disraeli\'s policies of territorial aggrandizement, military pomp, and imperial symbolism (such as making the Queen Empress of India), saying it did not fit a modern commercial and Christian nation.\n</p><p>In foreign policy he is best known for battling and besting Russia. Disraeli\'s second term was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Question" title="Eastern Question">Eastern Question</a>\xe2\x80\x94the slow decay of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and the desire of Russia, to gain at its expense. Disraeli arranged for the British to purchase a major interest in the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal_Company" title="Suez Canal Company">Suez Canal Company</a> (in Ottoman-controlled Egypt). In 1878, faced with Russian victories against the Ottomans, he worked at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> to maintain peace in the Balkans and made terms favourable to Britain which weakened Russia, its longstanding enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Disraeli\'s old reputation as the "Tory democrat" and promoter of the welfare state has faded as historians argue that he had few proposals for social legislation in 1874\xe2\x80\x9380, and that the 1867 Reform Act did not reflect a vision for the unenfranchised working man.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> However he did work to reduce class antagonism, for as Perry notes, "When confronted with specific problems, he sought to reduce tension between town and country, landlords and farmers, capital and labour, and warring religious sects in Britain and Ireland\xe2\x80\x94in other words, to create a unifying synthesis."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gladstone">Gladstone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Gladstone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/170px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/255px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/340px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 2x" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="457" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gladstone</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gladstonian_liberalism" title="Gladstonian liberalism">Gladstonian liberalism</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> (1809\xe2\x80\x931898) was the Liberal counterpart to Disraeli, serving as <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Premiership of William Gladstone">prime minister four times (1868\xe2\x80\x9374, 1880\xe2\x80\x9385, 1886, and 1892\xe2\x80\x9394)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> He was the moral compass of the Liberal Party and is famous for his oratory, his religiosity, his liberalism, his rivalry with Disraeli, and for his poor relations with the Queen. Gladstone\'s first ministry saw many reforms including Disestablishment of the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Ballot_Act_1872" title="Ballot Act 1872">secret voting</a>. His party was defeated in 1874, but made a comeback based on opposition to Turkey\'s Bulgarian atrocities against Christians. Gladstone\'s <a href="/wiki/Midlothian_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Midlothian Campaign">Midlothian Campaign</a> of 1879\xe2\x80\x9380 was an pathbreaking introduction of many modern political campaigning techniques. His Liberal party was increasingly pulled apart on the Irish issue. He proposed Irish <a href="/wiki/Home_rule" title="Home rule">home rule</a> in 1886; It failed to pass and the resulting split in the Liberal Party kept it out of office for most of the next 20 years.\n</p><p>Gladstone\'s financial policies, based on the notion of balanced budgets, low taxes and <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a>, were suited to a developing capitalist society but could not respond effectively as economic and social conditions changed. Called the "Grand Old Man" later in life, he was always a dynamic popular orator who appealed strongly to British workers and lower middle class. The deeply religious Gladstone brought a new moral tone to politics with his evangelical sensibility and opposition to aristocracy. His moralism often angered his upper-class opponents (including Queen Victoria, who strongly favoured Disraeli), and his heavy-handed control split the Liberal party. His foreign policy goal was to create a European order based on cooperation rather than conflict and mutual trust instead of rivalry and suspicion; the rule of law was to supplant the reign of force and self-interest. This Gladstonian concept of a harmonious <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a> was opposed to and ultimately defeated by the Germans with a <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarckian</a> system of manipulated alliances and antagonisms.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regarding Ireland, the major Liberal efforts focused on land reform, where the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Land_Acts" title="Irish Land Acts">ended centuries of landlord oppression</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/State_religion#Disestablishment" title="State religion">disestablishment</a> of the (Anglican) <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Church_Act_1869" title="Irish Church Act 1869">Irish Church Act 1869</a>.  Gladstone became a champion of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Home Rule</a>, but it caused a deep split in the Liberal Party. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> formed the breakaway <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist_Party" title="Liberal Unionist Party">Liberal Unionist Party</a> that refused to consider independence for Ireland and became allied with the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p><p>In terms of historic reforms, Gladstone\'s first ministry 1868\xe2\x80\x9374 was his most successful.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>  He was an idealist who insisted that government should take the lead in making society more efficient, more fair, and that the government should expand its role in society in order to extend liberty and toleration.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Elementary_Education_Act_1870" title="Elementary Education Act 1870">Education Act of 1870</a> made universal schooling a major national policy.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The judiciary system was made up of multiple overlapping and conflicting courts dating back centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Judicature_Act_1873" title="Supreme Court of Judicature Act 1873">Judicature Act of 1873</a> merged them into one central court.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In local government, the challenge of sanitation and clean water in fast-growing cities were met with new powers in the realm of public health. Local government was streamlined in a later Gladstone ministry, and made more powerful and standardized. Patronage and favoritism was replaced by civil service examinations, downplaying the role of family and aristocracy and emphasizing the role of talent and ability. The secret ballot was enacted in 1872 to prevent the buying of votes\xe2\x80\x94politicians would not pay out the money if they were not sure how the person voted. The <a href="/wiki/Trade_Union_Act_1871" title="Trade Union Act 1871">Trade Union Act of 1871</a> lessened the intimidation of employers, made unions legal, and protected their funding from lawsuits. The Protestant <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>, serving a small minority of rich landowners, was disestablished. Catholics no longer had to pay taxes to it.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> While the Navy was in fine shape, the Army was not.  Its organization was confused, its policies unfair, and its cruel punishments were based chiefly on brutal flogging. At the county level, politicians name the officers of the county militia units, preferring connections in class over capacity. The regular army called for enlistments for 21 years, but with reforms initiated by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Cardwell" title="Edward Cardwell">Edward Cardwell</a>, Gladstone\'s secretary for war, enlistments were reduced to six years, plus six years in reserve. Regiments were organized by territorial districts, and advanced with modern rifles. The complex chain of command was simplified, and in wartime the county militias were under the control of the central war office. Commissions of officer commissions were abolished, and flogging in peacetime was also abolished. The reforms were not quite complete, the Royal Duke of Cambridge still had great authority, despite his mediocre abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Historians have given Gladstone high marks on his successful reform program.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Salisbury">Salisbury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Salisbury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Historians agree that <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> (1830\xe2\x80\x931903) as foreign minister and prime minister in the late 19th century was a strong and effective leader in foreign affairs. He had a superb grasp of the issues, and proved:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>a patient, pragmatic practitioner, with a keen understanding of Britain\'s historic interests....He oversaw the partition of Africa, the emergence of Germany and the United States as imperial powers, and the transfer of British attention from the Dardanelles to Suez without provoking a serious confrontation of the great powers.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Historians portray Lord Salisbury as a talented leader who was an icon of traditional, aristocratic conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blake,_Baron_Blake" title="Robert Blake, Baron Blake">Robert Blake</a> considers Salisbury "a great foreign minister, [but] essentially negative, indeed reactionary in home affairs".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Professor P.T. Marsh\'s estimate is more favourable than Blake\'s, he says Salisbury was a leader who "held back the popular tide for twenty years."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Professor Paul Smith argues that, "into the \xe2\x80\x98progressive\xe2\x80\x99 strain of modern Conservatism he simply will not fit."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Professor H.C.G. Matthew points to "the narrow cynicism of Salisbury".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> One admirer of Salisbury, Maurice Cowling largely agrees with the critics and says Salisbury found the democracy born of the 1867 and 1884 Reform Acts as "perhaps less objectionable than he had expected\xe2\x80\x94succeeding, through his public persona, in mitigating some part of its nastiness."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_20th_century_1901.E2.80.931918"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century_1901\xe2\x80\x931918">Early 20th century 1901\xe2\x80\x931918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Early 20th century 1901\xe2\x80\x931918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Prime Ministers from 1900 to 1945: <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Marquess of Salisbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> was in power 1906\xe2\x80\x931914 (when it formed a wartime coalition). It passed the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms" title="Liberal welfare reforms">welfare reforms</a> that created a basic British welfare state. It weakened the veto power of Lords, blocked woman suffrage. In 1914 it apparently "solved" the problem of Irish Home Rule but when the war broke out the solution was shelved. <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> was Liberal Prime Minister between 1908 and 1916, followed by <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, 1916\xe2\x80\x9322. Although Asquith was the Party leader, the dominant Liberal was Lloyd George. Asquith was overwhelmed by the wartime role of coalition prime minister, and Lloyd George replaced him as the coalition prime minister in late 1916 but Asquith remained Liberal party leader. The two fought for years over control of the party, badly weakening it in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Martin_Pugh_(author)" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Pugh (author)">Martin Pugh</a> in <i>The Oxford Companion to British History</i> argues that Lloyd George:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>made a greater impact on British public life than any other 20th-century leader, thanks to his pre-war introduction of Britain\'s social welfare system (especially medical insurance, unemployment insurance, and old-age pensions, largely paid for by taxes on high incomes and on the land). Furthermore, in foreign affairs he played a leading role in winning the First World War, redrawing the map of Europe at the peace conference, and partitioning Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Edwardian_era_1901.E2.80.931914"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edwardian_era_1901\xe2\x80\x931914">Edwardian era 1901\xe2\x80\x931914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Edwardian era 1901\xe2\x80\x931914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian era</a></div>\n<p>Queen Victoria died in 1901 and her son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Edward VII</a> became king, inaugurating the Edwardian Era, which was characterised by great and ostentatious displays of wealth in contrast to the sombre Victorian Era. With the advent of the 20th century, things such as motion pictures, automobiles, and aeroplanes were coming into use. The new century was characterised by a feeling of great optimism. The social reforms of the last century continued into the 20th with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> being formed in 1900. Edward died in 1910, to be succeeded by <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a>, who reigned 1910\xe2\x80\x9336. Scandal-free, hard working and popular, George V was the British monarch who, with Queen Mary, established the modern pattern of exemplary conduct for British royalty, based on middle-class values and virtues. He understood the overseas Empire better than any of his prime ministers and used his exceptional memory for figures and details, whether of uniforms, politics, or relations, to good effect in reaching out in conversation with his subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The era was prosperous but political crises were escalating out of control. Dangerfield (1935) identified the "strange death of liberal England" as the multiple crisis that hit simultaneously in 1910\xe2\x80\x931914 with serious social and political instability arising from the Irish crisis, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Labour_Unrest&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Great Labour Unrest (page does not exist)">labor unrest</a>, the women\'s suffrage movements, and partisan and constitutional struggles in Parliament. At one point it even seemed the Army might refuse orders dealing with Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> No solution appeared in sight when the unexpected outbreak of the Great War in 1914 put domestic issues on hold.\n</p><p>McKibben argues that the political party system of the Edwardian era was in delicate balance on the eve of the war in 1914. The Liberals were in power with a progressive alliance of Labour and, off and on, Irish Nationalists. The coalition was committed to free trade (as opposed to the high tariffs the Conservatives sought), free collective bargaining for trades unions (which Conservatives opposed), an active social policy that was forging the welfare state, and constitutional reform to reduce the power of the House of Lords. The coalition lacked a long-term plan, because it was cobbled together from leftovers from the 1890s. The sociological basis was non-Anglican religion and non-English ethnicity rather than the emerging class conflict emphasized by Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">First World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: First World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_entry_into_World_War_I" title="British entry into World War I">British entry into World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="History of the United Kingdom during the First World War">History of the United Kingdom during the First World War</a></div>\n<p>On 4 August, the King declared war on Germany and Austria, following the advice of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>. The rest of the Empire automatically followed. The cabinet\'s basic reasons for declaring war focused on a deep commitment to France and avoidance of splitting the Liberal Party. Top Liberals led by Asquith and Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Edward Grey</a> threatened to resign if the cabinet refused to support France. That would deeply split the party and mean loss of control of the government to a coalition or to the Unionist (ie Conservative) opposition.  However, the large antiwar element among Liberals, with David Lloyd George as spokesperson, would support the war to honour the 1839 treaty that guaranteed Belgian neutrality. So Belgium rather than France was the public reason given.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/240px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" width="240" height="365" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/360px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/480px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1842" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Scrap of Paper \xe2\x80\x93 Enlist Today", 1914 British propaganda poster emphasizes German contempt for the 1839 treaty that guaranteed Belgian neutrality as merely a "scrap of paper" that Germany would ignore.</div></div></div>\n<p>Therefore the public reason given out by the government. and used in posters, was that Britain was required to safeguard Belgium\'s neutrality under the 1839 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a>. \n</p><p>Britain entered the war because of its implicit support for France, which had entered to support Russia, which in turn had entered to support Serbia. Britain became part of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Triple Entente</a> with France and Russia, which (with smaller allies) fought the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> of Germany, Austria and the Ottoman Empire. After a few weeks the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> turned into a killing ground in which millions of men died but no army made a large advance.  The main British contribution was financial\xe2\x80\x94loans and grants helped Russia, Italy and smaller allies afford the war.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The stalemate required an endless supply of men and munitions. By 1916, volunteering fell off, the government imposed conscription in Britain (but not in Ireland) to keep up the strength of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_World_War_I" title="British Army during World War I">Army</a>. After a rough start in industrial mobilisation, Britain replaced prime minister Asquith in December 1916 with the much more dynamic Liberal leader <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>. The nation now successfully mobilised its manpower, womanpower, industry, finances, Empire and diplomacy, in league with France and the U.S. to defeat the enemy. After defeating Russia, the Germans tried to win in the spring of 1918 before the millions of American soldiers arrived. They failed, and they were overwhelmed and finally accepted an Armistice in November 1918, that amounted to a surrender.\n</p><p>Britain eagerly supported the war, but in Ireland the Nationalists were restless and plotted a rebellion in 1916. It failed but the brutal repression that followed turned that element against Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arthur_Marwick_1965_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur_Marwick_1965-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The economy grew about 14% from 1914 to 1918 despite the absence of so many men in the services; by contrast the German economy shrank 27%. The War saw a decline of civilian consumption, with a major reallocation to munitions. The government share of GDP soared from 8% in 1913 to 38% in 1918 (compared to 50% in 1943).<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The war forced Britain to use up its financial reserves and borrow large sums from New York banks. After the U.S. entered in April 1917, the Treasury borrowed directly from the U.S. government.\n</p><p>The Royal Navy dominated the seas, defeating the smaller German fleet in the only major naval battle of the war, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a> in 1916. Germany was blockaded, leading to an increasing shortage short of food. Germany\'s naval strategy increasingly turned towards use of <a href="/wiki/U-Boats" class="mw-redirect" title="U-Boats">U-Boats</a> to strike back against the British, despite the risk of triggering war with the powerful neutral power, the United States. The waters around Britain were declared a war zone where any ship, neutral or otherwise, was a target. After the liner <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">Lusitania</a> was sunk in May 1915, drowning over 100 American passengers, protests by the United States led Germany to abandon unrestricted submarine warfare. With victory over Russia in 1917, Germany now calculated it could finally have numerical superiority on the Western Front. Planning for a massive spring offensive in 1918, it resumed the sinking of all merchant ships without warning. The US entered the war alongside the Allies (without actually joining them), and provided the needed money and supplies to sustain the Allies\' war efforts. The U-boat threat was ultimately defeated by a convoy system across the Atlantic.\n</p><p>On other fronts, the British, French, Australians, and Japanese seized Germany\'s colonies. Britain fought the Ottoman Empire, suffering defeats in the <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, while arousing the Arabs who helped expel the Turks from their lands. Exhaustion and <a href="/wiki/War-weariness" title="War-weariness">war-weariness</a> were growing worse in 1917, as the fighting in France continued with no end in sight. The German spring offensives of 1918 failed, and with the summer arrival of American soldiers at a rate of 10,000 per day,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> the Germans realised they were being overwhelmed. Germany agreed to a surrender on 11 November 1918.\n</p><p>Victorian attitudes and ideals that had continued into the first years of the 20th century changed during the First World War. The army had traditionally never been a large employer in the nation, with the regular army standing at 247,432 at the start of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Tibus_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tibus-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> By 1918, there were about five million people in the army and the fledgling <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>, newly formed from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> (RNAS) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> (RFC), was about the same size of the pre-war army. The almost three million casualties were known as the "lost generation", and such numbers inevitably left society scarred; but even so, some people felt their sacrifice was little regarded in Britain, with poems like <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Sassoon" title="Siegfried Sassoon">Siegfried Sassoon</a>\'s <i>Blighters</i> criticising the ill-informed <a href="/wiki/Jingoism" title="Jingoism">jingoism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Home_front" title="Home front">home front</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_settlement">Postwar settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Postwar settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The war had been won by Britain and its allies, but at a terrible human and financial cost, creating a sentiment that wars should never be fought again. The <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> was founded with the idea that nations could resolve their differences peacefully, but these hopes were unfulfilled. The harsh peace settlement imposed on Germany would leave it embittered and seeking revenge.\n</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> of 1919, Lloyd George, American President Woodrow Wilson and French premier Georges Clemenceau made all the major decisions. They formed the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> as a mechanism to prevent future wars. They sliced up the losers to form new nations in Europe, and divided up the German colonies and Ottoman holdings outside Turkey. They imposed what appeared to be heavy financial reparations (but in the event were of modest size). They humiliated Germany by forcing it to declare its guilt for starting the war, a policy that caused deep resentment in Germany and helped fuel reactions such as Nazism. Britain gained the German colony of <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika" title="Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a> and part of <a href="/wiki/Togoland" title="Togoland">Togoland</a> in Africa, while its dominions added other colonies. Britain gained League of Nations mandates over Palestine, which had been partly promised as a homeland for Jewish settlers, and Iraq. Iraq became fully independent in 1932. Egypt, which had been a British protectorate since 1882, became independent in 1922, although the British remained there until 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_independence_and_partition">Irish independence and partition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Irish independence and partition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_bills" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule bills">Irish Home Rule bills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Partition of Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Irish Declaration of Independence">Irish Declaration of Independence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a></div>\n<p>In 1912, the House of Lords managed to delay a Home Rule bill passed by the House of Commons. It was enacted as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Government of Ireland Act 1914</a>. Civil war threatened when the Protestant-Unionists of Northern Ireland refused to be placed under Catholic-Nationalist control.  Semi-military units were formed ready to fight\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Unionism">Unionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">Ulster Volunteers</a> opposed to the Act and their Nationalist counterparts, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a> supporting the Act. The outbreak of the World War in 1914 put the crisis on political hold. A disorganized <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> in 1916 was brutally suppressed by the British, which had the effect of galvanizing Nationalist demands for independence. Prime Minister Lloyd George failed to introduce Home Rule in 1918 and in the December 1918 General Election <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> won a majority of Irish seats. Its MPs refused to take their seats at Westminster, instead choosing to sit in the <a href="/wiki/First_D%C3%A1il" title="First D\xc3\xa1il">First D\xc3\xa1il</a> parliament in Dublin. A declaration of independence was ratified by <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann_(Irish_Republic)" title="D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann (Irish Republic)">D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann</a>, the self-declared Republic\'s parliament in January 1919. An <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish War">Anglo-Irish War</a> was fought between Crown forces and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1917%E2%80%9322)" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Republican Army (1917\xe2\x80\x9322)">Irish Republican Army</a> between January 1919 and June 1921. The war ended with the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> of December 1921 that established the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Six northern, predominantly Protestant counties became <a href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Ireland" title="History of Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and have remained part of the United Kingdom ever since, despite demands of the Catholic minority to unite with the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Britain officially adopted the name "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland" by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_and_Parliamentary_Titles_Act_1927" title="Royal and Parliamentary Titles Act 1927">Royal and Parliamentary Titles Act 1927</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Interwar_era_1918.E2.80.931939"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Interwar_era_1918\xe2\x80\x931939">Interwar era 1918\xe2\x80\x931939</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Interwar era 1918\xe2\x80\x931939">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar Britain</a></div>\n<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Marwick" title="Arthur Marwick">Arthur Marwick</a> sees a radical transformation of British society resulting from the Great War, a deluge that swept away many old attitudes and brought in a more equalitarian society. He sees the famous literary pessimism of the 1920s as misplaced, arguing there were major positive long-term consequences of the war to British society. He points to an energized self-consciousness among workers that quickly built up the Labour Party, the coming of partial woman suffrage, and an acceleration of social reform and state control of the economy. He sees a decline of deference toward the aristocracy and established authority in general, and the weakening among youth of traditional restraints on individual moral behavior. The chaperone faded away; village druggists sold contraceptives. Marwick says that class distinctions softened, national cohesion increased, and British society became more equal.<sup id="cite_ref-Arthur_Marwick_1965_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur_Marwick_1965-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">Popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As a leisure, literacy, wealth, ease of travel, and a broadened sense of community grew in Britain from the late 19th century onward, there was more time and interest in leisure activities of all sorts, on the part of all classes.<sup id="cite_ref-Peter_J._Beck_2008_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J._Beck_2008-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The annual vacation became common. Tourists flocked to seaside resorts; <a href="/wiki/Blackpool" title="Blackpool">Blackpool</a> hosted 7 million visitors a year in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Organized leisure was primarily a male activity, with middle-class women allowed in at the margins. There were class differences with upper-class clubs, and working-class and middle-class pubs.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Heavy drinking declined; there were more competitions that attracted heavy betting. Participation in sports and all sorts of leisure activities increased for the average Englishman, and his interest in spectator sports increased dramatically. By the 1920s the cinema and radio attracted all classes, ages and genders in very large numbers, with young women taking the lead.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Working-class men wearing flat caps and eating fish and chips were boisterous football spectators. They sang along at the music hall, fancied their pigeons, gambled on horse racing, and took the family to Blackpool in summer. The cartoon realization of this life style <i><a href="/wiki/Andy_Capp" title="Andy Capp">Andy Capp</a></i> began in 1957. Political activists complained that working-class leisure diverted men away from revolutionary agitation.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema_and_radio">Cinema and radio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Cinema and radio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitchcock,_Alfred_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/220px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg" width="220" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/330px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/440px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="802" data-file-height="890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitchcock,_Alfred_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Film director Alfred Hitchcock, 1955</div></div></div>\n<p>The British film industry emerged in the 1890s when cinemas in general broke through in the western world, and built heavily on the strong reputation of the London legitimate theatre for actors, directors and producers.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The problem was that the American market was so much larger and richer. It bought up the top talent, especially when Hollywood came to the fore in the 1920s and produced over 80 percent of the total world output. Efforts to fight back were futile\xe2\x80\x94the government set a quota for British made films, but it failed. Hollywood furthermore dominated the lucrative Canadian and Australian markets. <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> (based in Bombay) dominated the huge Indian market.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The most prominent directors remaining in London were <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Korda" title="Alexander Korda">Alexander Korda</a>, an expatriate Hungarian, and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a>. There was a revival of creativity in the 1933\xe2\x80\x9345 era, especially with the arrival of Jewish filmmakers and actors fleeing the Nazis.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, giant palaces were built for the huge audiences that wanted to see Hollywood films. In Liverpool 40 percent of the population attended one of the 69 cinemas once a week; 25 percent went twice. Traditionalists grumbled about the American cultural invasion, but the permanent impact was minor.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In radio, British audiences had no choice apart from the upscale programming of the BBC, a government agency which had a monopoly on broadcasting. <a href="/wiki/John_Reith,_1st_Baron_Reith" title="John Reith, 1st Baron Reith">John Reith</a> (1889\xe2\x80\x931971), an intensely moralistic engineer, was in full charge. His goal was to broadcast, "All that is best in every department of human knowledge, endeavour and achievement.... The preservation of a high moral tone is obviously of paramount importance."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Sport in the United Kingdom">Sport in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The British showed a more profound interest in sports, and in greater variety, than any rival. They gave pride of place to such moral issues as sportsmanship and fair play.<sup id="cite_ref-Peter_J._Beck_2008_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J._Beck_2008-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Football proved highly attractive to the urban working classes, which introduced the rowdy spectator to the sports world.  New games became popular almost overnight, including golf, lawn tennis, cycling and hockey. Women were much more likely to enter these sports than the old established ones. The aristocracy and landed gentry, with their ironclad control over land rights, dominated hunting, shooting, fishing and horse racing.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>  Cricket reflected the Imperial spirit throughout the Empire (except Canada).  Test matches began by the 1870s; the most famous are those between Australia and England for <a href="/wiki/The_Ashes" title="The Ashes">The Ashes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reading">Reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>As literacy and leisure time expanded after 1900, reading became a popular pastime. New additions to adult fiction doubled during the 1920s, reaching 2800 new books a year by 1935. Libraries tripled their stock, and saw heavy demand for new fiction.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> A dramatic innovation was the inexpensive paperback, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Allen_Lane" title="Allen Lane">Allen Lane</a> (1902\xe2\x80\x9370) at <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Books" title="Penguin Books">Penguin Books</a> in 1935. The first titles included novels by Ernest Hemingway and Agatha Christie. They were sold cheap (usually sixpence) in a wide variety of inexpensive stores such as Woolworth\'s.  Penguin aimed at an educated middle class "middlebrow" audience. It avoided the downscale image of American paperbacks. The line signaled cultural self-improvement and political education.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> However the war years caused a shortage of staff for publishers and book stores, and a severe shortage of rationed paper, worsened by the air raid on <a href="/wiki/Paternoster_Square" title="Paternoster Square">Paternoster Square</a> in 1940 that burned 5 million books in warehouses.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Romantic fiction was especially popular, with <a href="/wiki/Mills_and_Boon" class="mw-redirect" title="Mills and Boon">Mills and Boon</a> the leading publisher.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Romantic encounters were embodied in a principle of sexual purity that demonstrated not only social conservatism, but also how heroines could control their personal autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Adventure magazines became quite popular, especially those published by <a href="/wiki/DC_Thomson" title="DC Thomson">DC Thomson</a>; the publisher sent observers around the country to talk to boys and learn what they wanted to read about. The story line in magazines and cinema that most appealed to boys was the glamorous heroism of British soldiers fighting wars that were exciting and just.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_economics_of_1920s">Politics and economics of 1920s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Politics and economics of 1920s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg/150px-Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg" width="150" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg/225px-Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg/300px-Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1022" data-file-height="1478" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanley_Baldwin_ggbain.35233.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a> was <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> Prime Minister between 1923\xe2\x80\x931924, 1924\xe2\x80\x931929 and 1935\xe2\x80\x931937.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Expanding_the_welfare_state">Expanding the welfare state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Expanding the welfare state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Two major programs that permanently expanded the welfare state passed in 1919 and 1920 with surprisingly little debate, even as the Conservatives dominated parliament. The <a href="/wiki/Housing,_Town_Planning,_%26c._Act_1919" title="Housing, Town Planning, &amp;c. Act 1919">Housing, Town Planning, &amp;c. Act 1919</a> set up a system of government housing that followed the 1918 campaign promises of "homes fit for heroes." This "Addison Act", named after the first Minister of Health, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Addison" class="mw-redirect" title="Christopher Addison">Christopher Addison</a>, required local authorities to survey their housing needs, and start building houses to replace slums. The treasury subsidized the low rents. In England and Wales, 214,000 houses were built, and the Ministry of Health became largely a ministry of housing.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_Insurance_Act_1920" title="Unemployment Insurance Act 1920">Unemployment Insurance Act 1920</a> passed at a time of very little unemployment.
