It set up the dole system that provided 39 weeks of unemployment benefits to practically the entire civilian working population except domestic service, farm workers, and civil servants. Funded in part by weekly contributions from both employers and employed, it provided weekly payments of 15s for unemployed men and 12s for unemployed women. Historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mowat" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Mowat">Charles Mowat</a> calls these two laws "Socialism by the back door", and notes how surprised politicians were when the costs to the Treasury soared during the high unemployment of 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_control">Conservative control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Conservative control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_ministry" title="Lloyd George ministry">Lloyd George ministry</a> fell apart in 1922. <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, as leader of the Conservative Party (1923\xe2\x80\x9337) and as Prime Minister (in 1923\xe2\x80\x9324, 1924\xe2\x80\x9329 and 1935\xe2\x80\x9337), dominated British politics.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> His mixture of strong social reforms and steady government proved a powerful election combination, with the result that the Conservatives governed Britain either by themselves or as the leading component of the <a href="/wiki/UK_National_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="UK National Government">National Government</a>. He was the last party leader to win over 50% of the vote (in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">general election of 1931</a>). Baldwin\'s political strategy was to polarize the electorate so that voters would choose between the Conservatives on the right and the Labour Party on the left, squeezing out the Liberals in the middle.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The polarization did take place and while the Liberals remained active under Lloyd George, they won few seats and were a minor factor until they <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">joined a coalition with the Conservatives</a> in 2010. Baldwin\'s reputation soared in the 1920s and 1930s, but crashed after 1945 as he was blamed for the appeasement policies toward Germany, and as admirers of Churchill made him the Conservative icon. Since the 1970s Baldwin\'s reputation has recovered somewhat.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Labour won the 1923 election, but in 1924 Baldwin and the Conservatives returned with a large majority.\n</p><p>McKibbin finds that the political culture of the interwar period was built around an anti-socialist middle class, supported by the Conservative leaders, especially Baldwin.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Taxes rose sharply during the war and never returned to their old levels. A rich man paid 8% of his income in taxes before the war, and about a third afterwards. Much of the money went for the dole, the weekly unemployment benefits. About 5% of the national income every year was transferred from the rich to the poor. Taylor argues most people "were enjoying a richer life than any previously known in the history of the world: longer holidays, shorter hours, higher real wages."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British economy was lackluster in the 1920s, with sharp declines and high unemployment in heavy industry and coal, especially in Scotland and Wales. Exports of coal and steel fell in half by 1939 and the business community was slow to adopt the new labour and management principles coming from the US, such as <a href="/wiki/Fordism" title="Fordism">Fordism</a>, consumer credit, eliminating surplus capacity, designing a more structured management, and using greater economies of scale.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> For over a century the shipping industry had dominated world trade, but it remained in the doldrums despite various stimulus efforts by the government. With the very sharp decline in world trade after 1929, its condition became critical.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> put Britain back on the gold standard in 1925, which many economists blame for the mediocre performance of the economy. Others point to a variety of factors, including the inflationary effects of the World War and supply-side shocks caused by reduced working hours after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the late 1920s, economic performance had stabilised, but the overall situation was disappointing, for Britain had fallen behind the United States as the leading industrial power. There also remained a strong economic divide between the north and south of England during this period, with the south of England and the Midlands fairly prosperous by the Thirties, while parts of south Wales and the industrial north of England became known as "distressed areas" due to particularly high rates of unemployment and poverty. Despite this, the standard of living continued to improve as local councils built <a href="/wiki/Council_house" title="Council house">new houses</a> to let to families rehoused from outdated <a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slums</a>, with up to date facilities including indoor toilets, bathrooms and electric lighting now being included in the new properties. The private sector enjoyed a housebuilding boom during the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-Unstead_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unstead-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour">Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the war, <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unions">trade unions</a> were encouraged and their membership grew from 4.1&#160;million in 1914 to 6.5&#160;million in 1918. They peaked at 8.3&#160;million in 1920 before relapsing to 5.4&#160;million in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Coal was a sick industry; the best seams were being exhausted, raising the cost. Demand fell as oil began replacing coal for fuel. The <a href="/wiki/1926_United_Kingdom_general_strike" title="1926 United Kingdom general strike">1926 general strike</a> was a nine-day nationwide walkout of 1.3&#160;million railwaymen, transport workers, printers, dockers, iron workers and steelworkers supporting the 1.2&#160;million coal miners who had been locked out by the owners. The miners had rejected the owners\' demands for longer hours and reduced pay in the face of falling prices.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative government had provided a nine-month subsidy in 1925 but that was not enough to turn around a sick industry. To support the miners the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> (TUC), an umbrella organization of all trades unions, called out certain critical unions. The hope was the government would intervene to reorganize and rationalize the industry, and raise the subsidy. The Conservative government had stockpiled supplies and essential services continued with middle class volunteers. All three major parties opposed the strike. The Labour Party leaders did not approve and feared it would tar the party with the image of radicalism, for the Cominterm in Moscow had sent instructions for Communists to aggressively promote the strike. The general strike itself was largely non-violent, but the miners\' lockout continued and there was violence in Scotland. It was the only general strike in British history, for TUC leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a> considered it a mistake . Most historians treat it as a singular event with few long-term consequences, but Martin Pugh says it accelerated the movement of working-class voters to the Labour Party, which led to future gains.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Trade_Disputes_and_Trade_Unions_Act_1927" title="Trade Disputes and Trade Unions Act 1927">Trade Disputes and Trade Unions Act 1927</a> made general strikes illegal and ended the automatic payment of union members to the Labour Party. That act was largely repealed in 1946. The coal industry, used up the more accessible coal as costs rose output fell from 2567&#160;million tons in 1924 to 183 million in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The Labour government nationalised the mines in 1947.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Depression">Great Depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Great Depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Great Depression in the United Kingdom">Great Depression in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> originated in the United States in late 1929 and quickly spread to the world. Britain had never experienced the boom that had characterized the US, Germany, Canada and Australia in the 1920s, so its bust appeared less severe.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Britain\'s world trade fell in half (1929\xe2\x80\x9333), the output of heavy industry fell by a third, employment profits plunged in nearly all sectors. At the depth in summer 1932, registered unemployed numbered 3.5&#160;million, and many more had only part-time employment. Experts tried to remain optimistic. <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>, who had not predicted the slump, said, "\'There will be no serious direct consequences in London. We find the look ahead decidedly encouraging."<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doomsayers on the left such as Sidney and Beatrice Webb, J. A. Hobson, and G. D. H. Cole repeated the dire warnings they had been making for years about the imminent death of capitalism, only now far more people paid attention.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Starting in 1935 the <a href="/wiki/Left_Book_Club" title="Left Book Club">Left Book Club</a> provided a new warning every month, and built up the credibility of Soviet-style socialism as an alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Particularly hardest hit by economic problems were the north of England, Scotland, Northern Ireland and Wales; unemployment reached 70% in some areas at the start of the 1930s (with more than 3 million out of work nationally) and many families depended entirely on payments from local government known as the dole.\n</p><p>In 1936, by which time unemployment was lower, 200 unemployed men made a highly publicized march from Jarrow to London in a bid to show the plight of the industrial poor. Although much romanticized by the Left, the <a href="/wiki/Jarrow_Crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Jarrow Crusade">Jarrow Crusade</a> marked a deep split in the Labour Party and resulted in no government action.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Unemployment remained high until the war absorbed all the job seekers. <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>\'s book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Wigan_Pier" title="The Road to Wigan Pier">The Road to Wigan Pier</a></i> gives a bleak overview of the hardships of the time.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appeasement">Appeasement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Appeasement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Vivid memories of the horrors and deaths of the World War made Britain and its leaders strongly inclined to pacifism in the interwar era. The challenge came from dictators, first <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> of Italy, then <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> of a much more powerful <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. The <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> proved disappointing to its supporters; it was unable to resolve any of the threats posed by the dictators. British policy was to "appease" them in the hopes they would be satiated. By 1938 it was clear that war was looming, and that Germany had the world\'s most powerful military. The final act of appeasement came when Britain and France sacrificed Czechoslovakia to Hitler\'s demands at the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a> of 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Instead of satiation Hitler menaced Poland, and at last Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> dropped appeasement and stood firm in promising to defend Poland. Hitler however cut a deal with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> to divide Eastern Europe; when Germany did invade Poland in September 1939, Britain and France declared war; the British Commonwealth followed London\'s lead.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Second_World_War_1939.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x931945">Second World War 1939\xe2\x80\x931945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Second World War 1939\xe2\x80\x931945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_home_front_during_World_War_II" title="United Kingdom home front during World War II">United Kingdom home front during World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_United_Kingdom_home_front_during_World_War_II" title="Timeline of the United Kingdom home front during World War II">Timeline of the United Kingdom home front during World War II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of the United Kingdom during World War II">Military history of the United Kingdom during World War II</a></div>\n<p>The King declared war on <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in September 1939, after the German <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a>. During the quiet period of "<a href="/wiki/Phoney_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoney war">phoney war</a>", the British sent to France the most highly mechanized army in the world; together with France they had more tanks than Germany, but fewer warplanes. The smashing German victory in Spring 1940 was due entirely to "superior combat doctrine. Realistic training, imaginative battlefield leadership, and unparalleled initiative from generals down to sergeants."<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The British with the thinnest of margins <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">rescued its main army from</a> Dunkirk (as well as many French soldiers), leaving all their equipment and war supplies behind. <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> came to power, promising to fight the Germans to the very end. The Germans threatened an invasion\xe2\x80\x94which the Royal Navy was prepared to repel. First the Germans tried to achieve air supremacy but were defeated by the Royal Air Force in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> in late summer 1940. <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> declared war in December 1941, and quickly seized Hong Kong, Malaya, Singapore, and Burma, and threatened Australia and India. Britain formed an alliance with the Soviet Union (starting in 1941) and very close ties to the United States (starting in 1940). The war was very expensive. It was paid for by high taxes, by selling off assets, and by accepting large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Lend_Lease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lend Lease">Lend Lease</a> from the U.S. and Canada. The US gave $30&#160;billion in munitions; Canada also gave aid. (The American and Canadian aid did not have to be repaid, but there were also American loans that were repaid.)<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain\'s total mobilisation during this period proved to be successful in winning the war, by maintaining strong support from public opinion. The war was a "people\'s war" that enlarged democratic aspirations and produced promises of a postwar welfare state.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The media called it a "people\'s war"\xe2\x80\x94a term that caught on and signified the popular demand for planning and an expanded welfare state.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The Royal family played major symbolic roles in the war. They refused to leave London during the <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">Blitz</a> and were indefatigable in visiting troops, munition factories, dockyards, and hospitals all over the country.  All social classes appreciated how the royals shared the hopes, fears and hardships of the people.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobilisation_of_women">Mobilisation of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Mobilisation of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper,_metal,_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg/220px-INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg/330px-INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg/440px-INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper%2C_metal%2C_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2423" data-file-height="3640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:INF3-219_Salvage_Up_Housewives_and_at_em_-_put_out_your_paper,_metal,_bones_Artist_Yates-Wilson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salvage \xe2\x80\x93 "Up Housewives and at \'em! Put out your paper, metal, bones." Artist: Yates-Wilson.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historians credit Britain with a highly successful record of mobilising the home front for the war effort, in terms of mobilising the greatest proportion of potential workers, maximising output, assigning the right skills to the right task, and maintaining the morale and spirit of the people.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of this success was due to the systematic planned mobilisation of women, as workers, soldiers and housewives, enforced after December 1941 by conscription.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The women supported the war effort, and made the rationing of consumer goods a success. In some ways, the government over-responded, evacuating too many children in the first days of the war, closing cinemas as frivolous then reopening them when the need for cheap entertainment was clear, sacrificing cats and dogs to save a little space on shipping pet food, only to discover an urgent need to keep the rats and mice under control.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British relied successfully on voluntarism. Munitions production rose dramatically, and the quality remained high. Food production was emphasised, in large part to free shipping for munitions. Farmers increased the number of acres under cultivation from 12,000,000 to 18,000,000 (from about 50,000 to 75,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), and the farm labour force was expanded by a fifth, thanks especially to the Women\'s Land Army.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Welfare_state">Welfare state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Welfare state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Beveridge_Report" title="Beveridge Report">Beveridge Report</a></div>\n<p>The success of the government in providing new services, such as hospitals and school lunches, as well as egalitarian spirit, contributed to widespread support for an enlarged welfare state. It was supported by the coalition government and all major parties.  Welfare conditions, especially regarding food, improved during the war as the government imposed rationing and subsidized food prices. Conditions for housing worsened of course with the bombing, and clothing was in short supply.\n</p><p>Equality increased dramatically, as incomes declined sharply for the wealthy and for white collar workers, as their taxes soared, while blue collar workers benefited from rationing and price controls.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>People demanded an expansion of the welfare state as a reward to the people for their wartime sacrifices<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The goal was operationalized in a famous report by <a href="/wiki/William_Beveridge" title="William Beveridge">William Beveridge</a>. It recommended that the various income maintenance services that a grown-up piecemeal since 1911 be systematized and made universal. Unemployment benefits and sickness benefits were to be universal. There would be new benefits for maternity. The old-age pension system would be revised and expanded, and require that a person retired. A full-scale National Health Service would provide free medical care for everyone. All the major parties endorsed the principles and they were largely put into effect when peace returned.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar">Postwar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Postwar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Postwar_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Postwar Britain">Postwar Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="History of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">History of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a></div>\n<p>Britain had won the war, but it lost India in 1947 and nearly all the rest of the Empire by the 1960s. It debated its role in world affairs and joined the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1945, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in 1949, and became a close ally of the United States. Prosperity returned in the 1950s, and London remained a world centre of finance and culture, but the nation was no longer a major world power.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In 1973, after a long debate and initial rejection, it joined the <a href="/wiki/Common_Market" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Market">Common Market</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Austerity.2C_1945.E2.80.931950"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Austerity,_1945\xe2\x80\x931950">Austerity, 1945\xe2\x80\x931950</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Austerity, 1945\xe2\x80\x931950">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The end of the war saw a <a href="/wiki/Landslide_victory" title="Landslide victory">landslide victory</a> for <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>. They were elected on a manifesto of greater <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> with left-wing policies such as the creation of a <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a>, more <a href="/wiki/Council_housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Council housing">council housing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalisation">nationalisation</a> of several major industries. Britain faced a severe financial crisis, and responded by reducing her international responsibilities and by sharing the hardships of an "age of austerity".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Large loans from the United States and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> grants helped rebuild and modernise its infrastructure and business practices. <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom_during_and_after_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom during and after World War II">Rationing</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="National Service">conscription</a> dragged on well into the post war years, and the country suffered <a href="/wiki/British_winter_of_1946%E2%80%931947" class="mw-redirect" title="British winter of 1946\xe2\x80\x931947">one of the worst winters on record</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, morale was boosted by events such as the marriage of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">Princess Elizabeth</a> in 1947 and the <a href="/wiki/Festival_of_Britain" title="Festival of Britain">Festival of Britain</a> in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalisation">Nationalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Nationalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Labour Party experts went into the files to find the detailed plans for nationalisation. To their surprise<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, there were no plans. The leaders decided to act fast to keep up the momentum of the 1945 electoral landslide. They started with the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, civil aviation, coal, and <a href="/wiki/Cable_and_Wireless" class="mw-redirect" title="Cable and Wireless">Cable and Wireless</a>. Then came railways, canals, road haulage and trucking, electricity, and gas. Finally came iron and steel, which was a special case because it was a manufacturing industry. Altogether, about one fifth of the economy was nationalised. Labour dropped its plans to nationalise farmlands. The procedure used was developed by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Morrison" title="Herbert Morrison">Herbert Morrison</a>, who as <a href="/wiki/Lord_President_of_the_Council" title="Lord President of the Council">Lord President of the Council</a> chaired the Committee on the Socialization of Industries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He followed the model that had already been used to establish public corporations such as the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> (1927). In exchange for the shares, the owners of the companies were given government bonds paying low rates of interest, and the government took full ownership of each affected company, consolidating it into a national monopoly. The management remained the same, but they were now effectively civil servants working for the government.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto1_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the Labour Party leadership, nationalisation was a way to consolidate economic planning in their own hands. It was not designed to modernise old industries, make them efficient, or transform their organisational structure. There was no money for modernisation, although the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, operated separately by American planners, did force many British businesses to adopt modern managerial techniques. Hardline socialists were disappointed, as the nationalised industries seemed identical to the old private corporations, and national planning was made virtually impossible by the government\'s financial constraints. Socialism was in place, but it did not seem to make a major difference. Rank-and-file workers had long been motivated to support Labour by tales of the mistreatment of workers by foremen and the management. The foremen and the managers were the same people as before, with much the same power over the workplace. There was no worker control of industry. The unions resisted government efforts to set wages. By the time of the general elections in 1950 and 1951, Labour seldom boasted about nationalisation of industry. Instead it was the Conservatives who decried the inefficiency and mismanagement, and promised to reverse the takeover of steel and trucking.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto1_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosperity_of_the_postwar_years">Prosperity of the postwar years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Prosperity of the postwar years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As the country headed into the 1950s, rebuilding continued and a number of immigrants from the remaining <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, mostly the Caribbean and the Indian subcontinent, were invited to help the rebuilding effort. As the 1950s wore on, Britain lost its place as a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a> and could no longer maintain its large <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Empire</a>. This led to decolonisation, and a withdrawal from almost all of its colonies by 1970. Events such as the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> showed that the UK\'s status had fallen in the world. The 1950s and 1960s were, however, relatively prosperous times after the Second World War, and saw the beginning of a modernisation of the UK, with the construction of its first <a href="/wiki/Motorways" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorways">motorways</a> for example, and also during the 1960s a great cultural movement began which expanded across the world. Unemployment was relatively low during this period and the standard of living continued to rise with more new private and council housing developments taking place and the number of slum properties diminishing.\n</p><p>The postwar period also witnessed a dramatic rise in the average standard of living,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> as characterised by a 40% rise in average real wages from 1950 to 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Earnings for men in industry rose by 95% between 1951 and 1964, while during that same period the official workweek was reduced and five reductions in income tax were made.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Those in traditionally poorly paid semi-skilled and unskilled occupations saw a particularly marked improvement in their wages and living standards. As summed up by <a href="/wiki/R._J._Unstead" title="R. J. Unstead">R. J. Unstead</a>&#160;:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Opportunities in life, if not equal, were distributed much more fairly than ever before and\\ the weekly wage-earner, in particular, had gained standards of living that would have been almost unbelievable in the thirties."<sup id="cite_ref-Unstead_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unstead-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In 1950, the UK standard of living was higher than in any EEC country apart from Belgium. It was 50% higher than the West German standard of living, and twice as high as the Italian standard of living. By the earlier Seventies, however, the UK standard of living was lower than all EEC countries apart from Italy (which, according to one calculation, was roughly equal to Britain). In 1951, the average weekly earnings of men over the age of 21 stood at \xc2\xa38 6s 0d, and nearly doubled a decade later to \xc2\xa315 7s 0d. By 1966, average weekly earnings stood at \xc2\xa320 6s 0d.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Between 1964 and 1968, the percentage of households with a television set rose from 80.5% to 85.5%, a washing machine from 54% to 63%, a refrigerator from 35% to 55%, a car from 38% to 49%, a telephone from 21.5% to 28%, and central heating from 13% to 23%.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1951 and 1963, wages rose by 72% while prices rose by 45%, enabling people to afford more consumer goods than ever before.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Between 1955 and 1967, the average earnings of weekly-paid workers increased by 96% and those of salaried workers by 95%, while prices rose by about 45% in the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The rising affluence of the Fifties and Sixties was underpinned by sustained full employment and a dramatic rise in workers\' wages. In 1950, the average weekly wage stood at \xc2\xa36.8s, compared with \xc2\xa311.2s.6d in 1959. As a result of wage rises, consumer spending also increased by about 20% during this same period, while economic growth remained at about 3%. In addition, food rations were lifted in 1954 while hire-purchase controls were relaxed in the same year. As a result of these changes, large numbers of the working classes were able to participate in the consumer market for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> As noted by Harriet Wilson:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>National wealth has grown considerably, and although the shareout of this among the social classes has remained substantially of the same proportions, it has meant a considerable rise in the standard of living of all classes. It is estimated that in Britain at the turn of the century average earnings in industry sufficed merely to meet the essential needs of a two-child family, today average earnings allow the industrial wage-earner to spend a third of his income on things other than basic needs."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The significant real wage increases in the 1950s and 1960s contributed to a rapid increase in working-class consumerism, with British consumer spending rising by 45% between 1952 and 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In addition, entitlement to various fringe benefits was improved. In 1955, 96% of manual labourers were entitled to two weeks\xe2\x80\x99 holiday with pay, compared with 61% in 1951. By the end of the 1950s, Britain had become one of the world\'s most affluent countries, and by the early Sixties, most Britons enjoyed a level of prosperity that had previously been known only to a small minority of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> For the young and unattached, there was, for the first time in decades, spare cash for leisure, clothes, and luxuries. In 1959, <i><a href="/wiki/Queen_(magazine)" title="Queen (magazine)">Queen</a></i> magazine declared that "Britain has launched into an age of unparalleled lavish living." Average wages were high while jobs were plentiful, and people saw their personal prosperity climb even higher. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> claimed that "the luxuries of the rich have become the necessities of the poor." Levels of disposable income rose steadily,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> with the spending power of the average family rising by 50% between 1951 and 1979, and by the end of the Seventies, 6 out of 10 families had come to own a car.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As noted by Martin Pugh,\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Keynesian economic management enabled British workers to enjoy a golden age of full employment which, combined with a more relaxed attitude towards working mothers, led to the spread of the two-income family. Inflation was around 4 per cent, money wages rose from an average of \xc2\xa38 a week in 1951 to \xc2\xa315 a week by 1961, home-ownership spread from 35 per cent in 1939 to 47 per cent by 1966, and the relaxation of credit controls boosted the demand for consumer goods.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>By 1963, 82% of all private households had a television, 72% a vacuum cleaner, 45%a washing machine, and 30% a refrigerator. In addition, as noted by John Burnett,\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What was equally striking was that ownership of such things had spread down the social scale and the gap between professional and manual workers had considerably narrowed.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>A study of a slum area in Leeds (which was due for demolition) found that 74% of the households had a T.V., 41% a vacuum, and 38% a washing machine. In another slum area, St Mary\'s in Oldham (where in 1970 few of the houses had fixed baths or a hot water supply and half shared outside toilets), 67% of the houses were rated as comfortably furnished and a further 24% furnished luxuriously, with smart modern furniture, deep pile carpeting, and decorations.\n</p><p>The provision of household amenities steadily improved during the second half of the twentieth century. From 1971 to 1983, households having the sole use of a fixed bath or shower rose from 88% to 97%, and those with an internal WC from 87% to 97%. In addition, the number of households with central heating almost doubled during that same period, from 34% to 64%. By 1983, 94% of all households had a refrigerator, 81% a colour television, 80% a washing machine, 57% a deep freezer, and 28% a tumble-drier.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1950 and 1970, however, Britain was overtaken by most of the countries of the European Common Market in terms of the number of telephones, refrigerators, television sets, cars, and washing machines per 100 of the population (although Britain remained high in terms of bathrooms and lavatories per 100 people). Although the British standard of living was increasing, the standard of living in other countries increased faster.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> According to a 1968 study by Anthony Sampson, British workers:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In ten years, from having had a much higher standard of living than the continent, they have slipped right back. Taking the national income per head (a rough yardstick), the British by 1967 had sunk to eighth place among OECD countries, with an annual income of $1,910 compared with $2,010 for Germany, $2,060 for France and $2,480 for Switzerland: and Britain\'s falling position already shows itself in the lower proportion of new cars and new houses (though still leading with TV sets and washing machines)."<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In 1976, UK wages were amongst the lowest in Western Europe, being half of West German rates and two-thirds of Italian rates.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In addition, while educational opportunities for working-class people had widened significantly since the end of the Second World War, a number of developed countries came to overtake Britain in some educational indicators. By the early 1980s, some 80% to 90% of school leavers in France and West Germany received vocational training, compared with 40% in the United Kingdom. By the mid-1980s, over 80% of pupils in the United States and West Germany and over 90% in Japan stayed in education until the age of eighteen, compared with barely 33% of British pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, only 35% of 16- to 18-year-olds were in full-time education or training, compared with 80% in the United States, 77% in Japan, 69% in France, and 49% in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> There also remained gaps between manual and non-manual workers in areas such as fringe benefits and wage levels. In April 1978, for instance, male full-time manual workers aged 21 and above averaged a gross weekly wage of \xc2\xa380.70, while the equivalent for male white collar workers stood at \xc2\xa3100.70.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_to_Commonwealth">Empire to Commonwealth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Empire to Commonwealth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britain\'s control over its Empire loosened during the interwar period. <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">Nationalism</a> strengthened in other parts of the empire, particularly in India and in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.\n</p><p>Between 1867 and 1910, the UK had granted Australia, Canada, and New Zealand "Dominion" status (near complete autonomy within the Empire). They became charter members of the British Commonwealth of Nations (known as the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> since 1949), an informal but close-knit association that succeeded the British Empire. Beginning with the independence of India and Pakistan in 1947, the remainder of the British Empire was almost completely dismantled. Today, most of Britain\'s former colonies belong to the Commonwealth, almost all of them as independent members. There are, however, 13 former British colonies, including <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>, and others, which have elected to continue rule by London and are known as <a href="/wiki/British_overseas_territory" class="mw-redirect" title="British overseas territory">British Overseas Territories</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_the_Troubles_to_the_Belfast_Agreement">From the Troubles to the Belfast Agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: From the Troubles to the Belfast Agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">The Troubles</a></div>\n<p>In the 1960s, moderate unionist Prime Minister of Northern Ireland <a href="/wiki/Terence_O%27Neill" title="Terence O&#39;Neill">Terence O\'Neill</a> tried to reform the system and give a greater voice to Catholics who comprised 40% of the population of Northern Ireland. His goals were blocked by militant Protestants led by the Rev. <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The increasing pressures from nationalists for reform and from unionists to resist reform led to the appearance of the civil rights movement under figures like <a href="/wiki/John_Hume" title="John Hume">John Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austin_Currie" title="Austin Currie">Austin Currie</a> and others. Clashes escalated out of control as the army could barely contain the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA) and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Association" title="Ulster Defence Association">Ulster Defence Association</a>. British leaders feared their withdrawal would give a "Doomsday Scenario", with widespread communal strife, followed by the mass exodus of hundreds of thousands of refugees. London shut down Northern Ireland\'s parliament and began direct rule. By the 1990s, the failure of the IRA campaign to win mass public support or achieve its aim of a British withdrawal led to negotiations that in 1998 produced the \'<a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>\'. It won popular support and largely ended the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_economy_in_the_late_20th_century">The economy in the late 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: The economy in the late 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="History of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">History of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a></div>\n<p>After the relative prosperity of the 1950s and 1960s, the UK experienced extreme industrial strife and <a href="/wiki/Stagflation" title="Stagflation">stagflation</a> through the 1970s following a global economic downturn; Labour had returned to government in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964</a> under <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> to end 13 years of Conservative rule. The Conservatives were <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">restored to government</a> in 1970 under <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, who failed to halt the country\'s economic decline and was ousted in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">1974</a> as Labour returned to power under Harold Wilson. The economic crisis deepened following Wilson\'s return and things fared little better under his successor <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>.\n</p><p>A strict modernisation of its economy began under the controversial Conservative leader <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> following her <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">election as prime minister</a> in 1979, which saw a time of record <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> as <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Deindustrialisation">deindustrialisation</a> saw the end of much of the country\'s <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> industries but also <a href="/wiki/Post-World_War_II_boom" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-World War II boom">a time of economic boom</a> as stock markets became liberalised and State-owned industries became <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatised</a>. Her rise to power was seen as the symbolic end of the time in which the British economy had become the "sick man" of western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>\nInflation also fell during this period and trade union power was reduced.\n</p><p>However the <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%931985)" class="mw-redirect" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984\xe2\x80\x931985)">miners\' strike of 1984\xe2\x80\x931985</a> sparked the end of most of the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> mining. The exploitation of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> and oil brought in substantial tax and export revenues to aid the new economic boom. This was also the time that the IRA took the issue of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> to Great Britain, maintaining a prolonged bombing campaign on the British mainland.\n</p><p>After the economic boom of the 1980s a brief but severe recession occurred between 1990 and 1992 following the economic chaos of <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> under government of <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, who had succeeded Margaret Thatcher in 1990. However the rest of the 1990s saw the beginning of a period of continuous economic growth that lasted over 16 years and was greatly expanded under the <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> following his landslide election victory in 1997, with a rejuvenated party having abandoned its commitment to policies including nuclear disarmament and nationalisation of key industries, and no reversal of the Thatcher-led union reforms.\n</p><p>From 1964 up until 1996, income per head had doubled, while ownership of various household goods had significantly increased. By 1996, two-thirds of households owned cars, 82% had central heating, most people owned a VCR, and one in five houses had a home computer.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> In 1971, 9% of households had no access to a shower or bathroom, compared with only 1% in 1990; largely due to demolition or modernisation of older properties which lacked such facilities. In 1971, only 35% had central heating, while 78% enjoyed this amenity in 1990. By 1990, 93% of households had colour television, 87% had telephones, 86% had washing machines, 80% had deep-freezers, 60% had video-recorders, and 47% had microwave ovens. Holiday entitlements had also become more generous. In 1990, nine out of ten full-time manual workers were entitled to more than four weeks of paid holiday a year, while twenty years previously only two-thirds had been allowed three weeks or more.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The postwar period also witnessed significant improvements in housing conditions. In 1960, 14% of British households had no inside toilet, while in 1967 22% of all homes had no basic hot water supply. By the 1990s, most homes had these amenities together with central heating, which was a luxury just two decades before.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> From 1996/7 to 2006/7, real median household income increased by 20% while real mean household incomes increased by 23%. There has also been a shift towards a service-based economy in the years following the end of the Second World War, with 11% of working people employed in manufacturing in 2006, compared with 25% in 1971.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Common_Market_.28EEC.29.2C_then_EU.2C_membership"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_Market_(EEC),_then_EU,_membership">Common Market (EEC), then EU, membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Common Market (EEC), then EU, membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britain\'s wish to join the Common Market (as the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> was known in Britain) was first expressed in July 1961 by the Macmillan government. It was vetoed in 1963 by <a href="/wiki/French_President" class="mw-redirect" title="French President">French President</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> After initially hesitating over the issue, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>\'s Labour Government lodged the UK\'s second application (in May 1967) to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community">European Community</a>, as it was now called. Like the first, though, it was vetoed by de Gaulle.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1973, with DeGaulle gone, Conservative Prime Minister Heath negotiated terms for admission and Britain finally joined the Community. In opposition, the Labour Party was deeply divided, though its Leader, Harold Wilson, remained in favour. In the 1974 General Election, the Labour Party manifesto included a pledge to renegotiate terms for Britain\'s membership and then hold a referendum on whether to stay in the EC on the new terms. This was a constitutional procedure without precedent in British history. In the subsequent referendum campaign, rather than the normal British tradition of "collective responsibility", under which the government takes a policy position which all cabinet members are required to support publicly, members of the Government (and the Conservative opposition) were free to present their views on either side of the question. A <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_referendum,_1975" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom referendum, 1975">referendum</a> was duly held on 5 June 1975, and the proposition to continue membership was passed with a substantial majority.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Single_European_Act" title="Single European Act">Single European Act</a> (SEA) was the first major revision of the 1957 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome" title="Treaty of Rome">Treaty of Rome</a>. In 1987, the Conservative government under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> <a href="/wiki/European_Communities_(Amendment)_Act_1986" title="European Communities (Amendment) Act 1986">enacted it</a> into UK law.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a> transformed the European Community into the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. In 1992, the Conservative government under <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> ratified it, against the opposition of his backbench <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Rebels" title="Maastricht Rebels">Maastricht Rebels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a> introduced many changes to the treaties of the Union. Prominent changes included more <a href="/wiki/Qualified_majority_voting" class="mw-redirect" title="Qualified majority voting">qualified majority voting</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of Ministers</a>, increased involvement of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in the legislative process through extended <a href="/wiki/Codecision_procedure" class="mw-redirect" title="Codecision procedure">codecision</a> with the Council of Ministers, eliminating the <a href="/wiki/Three_pillars_of_the_European_Union" title="Three pillars of the European Union">pillar system</a> and the creation of a <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Council" title="President of the European Council">President of the European Council</a> with a term of two and a half years and a <a href="/wiki/High_Representative_of_the_Union_for_Foreign_Affairs_and_Security_Policy" title="High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy">High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy</a> to present a united position on EU policies. The Treaty of Lisbon will also make the Union\'s human rights charter, the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights</a>, legally binding. The Lisbon Treaty also leads to an increase in the voting weight of the UK in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon#Council" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Council of the European Union</a> from 8.4% to 12.4%. In July 2008, the Labour government under <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> approved the treaty and the Queen ratified it.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Devolution_for_Scotland_and_Wales">Devolution for Scotland and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Devolution for Scotland and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish devolution">Scottish devolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh devolution">Welsh devolution</a></div>\n<p>On 11 September 1997, (on the 700th anniversary of the Scottish victory over the English at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> was held on establishing a devolved <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>. This resulted in an overwhelming \'yes\' vote both to establishing the parliament and granting it limited tax varying powers. One week later, a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Welsh devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> in Wales on establishing a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> was also approved but with a very narrow majority. The first elections were held, and these bodies began to operate, in 1999. The creation of these bodies has widened the differences between the <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">Countries of the United Kingdom</a>, especially in areas like <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare in the United Kingdom">healthcare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> It has also brought to the fore the so-called <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian_question" title="West Lothian question">West Lothian question</a> which is a complaint that devolution for Scotland and Wales but not England has created a situation where Scottish and Welsh MPs in the UK Parliament can, in principle, vote on internal matters affecting England alone whereas English MPs have no say in similar matters affecting Scotland and Wales.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="War_in_Afghanistan_and_Iraq.2C_and_terrorist_attacks"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_in_Afghanistan_and_Iraq,_and_terrorist_attacks">War in Afghanistan and Iraq, and terrorist attacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: War in Afghanistan and Iraq, and terrorist attacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001 General Election</a>, the Labour Party won a second successive victory, though voter turnout dropped to the lowest level for more than 80 years.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, the <a href="/wiki/September_11th_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11th attacks">September 11th attacks</a> in the United States led to American President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> launching the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">invasion of</a> <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> aided by British troops in October 2001. Thereafter, with the US focus shifting to Iraq, Tony Blair convinced the Labour and Conservative MPs to vote in favour of supporting the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a>, despite huge anti-war marches held in London and Glasgow. Forty-six thousand British troops, one-third of the total strength of the Army\'s land forces, were deployed to assist with the invasion of Iraq and thereafter British armed forces were responsible for security in southern Iraq. All British forces were withdrawn in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Labour Party won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a> and a third consecutive term.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> On 7 July 2005, <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">a series of four suicide bombings</a> struck London, killing 52 commuters, in addition to the four bombers.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalist_government_in_Scotland">Nationalist government in Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Nationalist government in Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>2007 saw the first ever election victory for the pro-independence <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> (SNP) in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_Election,_2007" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament Election, 2007">Scottish Parliament elections</a>. They formed a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a> with plans to hold a referendum before 2011 to seek a mandate "to negotiate with the Government of the United Kingdom to achieve <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Independence">independence for Scotland</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Most opinion polls show minority support for independence, although support varies depending on the nature of the question. The response of the unionist parties was to establish the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Scottish_Devolution" title="Commission on Scottish Devolution">Calman Commission</a> to examine further <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> of powers,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> a position that had the support of the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Responding to the findings of the review, the UK government announced on 25 November 2009, that new powers would be devolved to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a>, notably on how it can raise tax and carry out capital borrowing, and the running of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament elections">Scottish Parliament elections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> These proposals were detailed in a <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a> setting out a <a href="/wiki/Scotland_Bill_2010%E2%80%9311" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotland Bill 2010\xe2\x80\x9311">new Scotland Bill</a>, to become law before the 2015 Holyrood elections.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> The proposal was criticised by the UK parliament opposition parties for not proposing to implement any changes before the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">next general election</a>. Scottish Constitution Minister <a href="/wiki/Michael_Russell_(politician)" title="Michael Russell (politician)">Michael Russell</a> criticised the white paper, calling it "flimsy" and stating that their proposed <a href="/wiki/Referendum_(Scotland)_Bill,_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="Referendum (Scotland) Bill, 2010">Referendum (Scotland) Bill, 2010</a>, whose own white paper was to be published five days later, would be "more substantial".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC25Nov09NewPowersAfterCalman-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>, the Calman Review white paper proposals fall short of what would normally be seen as requiring a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-Independent30Nov09SNPReveals_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Independent30Nov09SNPReveals-241">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_general_election,_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament general election, 2011">2011 election</a> saw a decisive victory for the SNP which was able to form a majority government intent on delivering a referendum on independence.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Within hours of the victory, Prime Minister David Cameron guaranteed that the UK government would not put any legal or political obstacles in the way of such a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Some unionist politicians, including former Labour First Minister <a href="/wiki/Henry_McLeish" title="Henry McLeish">Henry McLeish</a>, have responded to the situation by arguing that Scotland should be offered \'<a href="/wiki/Devo-max" class="mw-redirect" title="Devo-max">devo-max</a>\' as an alternative to independence,<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> and First Minister Alex Salmond has signalled his willingness to include it on the referendum ballot paper.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_2008_economic_crisis">The 2008 economic crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: The 2008 economic crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the wake of the global <a href="/wiki/Economic_crisis_of_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic crisis of 2008">economic crisis of 2008</a>, the United Kingdom economy contracted, experiencing negative economic growth throughout 2009. The announcement in November 2008 that the economy had shrunk for the first time since late 1992 brought an end to 16 years of continuous economic growth. Causes included an end to the easy credit of the preceding years, reduction in consumption and substantial depreciation of sterling (which fell 25% against the euro between January 2008 and January 2009),<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> leading to increased import costs, notably of oil.\n</p><p>On 8 October 2008, the <a href="/wiki/British_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="British Government">British Government</a> announced a <a href="/wiki/2008_United_Kingdom_bank_rescue_package" title="2008 United Kingdom bank rescue package">bank rescue package</a> of around \xc2\xa3500&#160;billion<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> ($850&#160;billion at the time). The plan comprised three parts.: \xc2\xa3200&#160;billion to be made available to the banks in the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>\'s Special Liquidity Scheme; the Government was to increase the banks\' market capitalization, through the Bank Recapitalization Fund, with an initial \xc2\xa325&#160;billion and another \xc2\xa325&#160;billion to be provided if needed; and the Government was to temporarily underwrite any eligible lending between British banks up to around \xc2\xa3250&#160;billion. With the UK officially coming out of recession in the fourth quarter of 2009\xe2\x80\x94ending six consecutive quarters of economic decline\xe2\x80\x94the Bank of England decided against further <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_easing" title="Quantitative easing">quantitative easing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_2010_coalition_government">The 2010 coalition government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: The 2010 coalition government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">United Kingdom General Election</a> of 6 May 2010 resulted in the first <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> since 1974, with the Conservative Party winning the largest number of seats, but falling short of the 326 seats required for an overall majority. Following this, the Conservatives and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> agreed to form the first <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">coalition government</a> for the UK since the end of the Second World War, with <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> becoming Prime Minister and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> Deputy Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the coalition government, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">British military aircraft</a> participated in the UN-mandated <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">intervention</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Libyan civil war">2011 Libyan civil war</a>, flying a total of 3,000 air <a href="/wiki/Sortie" title="Sortie">sorties</a> against forces loyal to the Libyan dictator <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> between March and October 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> 2011 also saw England suffer <a href="/wiki/2011_England_riots" title="2011 England riots">unprecedented rioting</a> in its major cities in early August, killing five people and causing over \xc2\xa3200&#160;million worth of property damage.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late October 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Heads_of_Government_Meeting_2011" title="Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting 2011">prime ministers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realms</a> voted to grant gender equality in the <a
